RATING: 6
1 play / 5 players

LIKE: I liked the strategy involved to try to position yourself for control of a nation. I enjoy games with player-controlled tempo.
DISLIKE: For advanced gamers. Can be cutthroat when another player takes something away from you. Tough to see some information on the cards and remember some minor rules. The high competitiveness felt draining.

OVERALL: This was a tough game for me to see the rhythm on a first play. I'm not as good seeing the positional strategy in games, so it took me a while to understand some of the right-hand, left-hand player order strategies. I liked the game, but there is much to remember. Not an everyday game, but one I would like to revisit.

RATING: 6.5
I need to play this again, BUT on the positive side it's deep, interesting, rather original and has high replayability.  However, on the negative side, the game situation is constantly emphemeral and is prone to both long downtime and bouts of Analysis Paralysis.  I'm not sure that first criticism will turn out to be a bad one - I may get to grips with it after more plays, and thus find it refreshing.  But to start with, the constantly changing situation can be rather hard to get your head around - especially if you're a wargamer used to linearity.

A friend of mine commented "the thing about this game is that to win you don't need to follow what's going on on the map in the second half of the game. Just look at the investments." Spot on. The more you play, the more you see this. Weird hey! A game that looks like an Empire game and yet all those ships and armies can be next to irrelevant at the end. 

The AP could be a serious problem and needs a house rule - we have used an egg timer for choosing investment chits - you get a minute for each choice, but may invoke the "I need 5 mins clause" once per game.  When you do this, the game loses nothing but is playable in under 2 hours.

I am looking forward to playing again, so we'll see how it's score changes.  I expect it is likely to be a game that I think more highly of as I get to know it better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Imperial is a very darkly themed economic game. Players take the roles of pre-WWI war profiteers, buying bonds in various empires with the intention of gaining a controlling interest in them and manipulating their governments into going to war with each other so you can profit further and expand your economic empire.

It is a fairly heavy game, and I have to confess that when I first started playing, I was very overwhelmed with the choices I could make and was very frustrated with not being able to figure out how my choices fit into the game as a whole. That said, after the first hour or so, my frustration and confusion was gone and I came to appreciate how elegant and novel a game Imperial is.

It's a tougher economic game, but I'm totally sold on it and hope to play again soon.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Imperial is a really mediocre game when played with the investor card and/or the random nation distribution.

The randomly distributed nations are passable in three palyers since you get complimentary pairs but with more you can get stuck with Austria-Hungary and Germany and not be able to make enough money to buy into an actual earner. Just play with the real rule of buying bonds to start. You can always buy into an earner but the other players probably got a head start.

The investor card is crappy. It creates these silly timing puzzles that may not even be solvable and artificially hands nations to other players. Who cares if you've been running Britain well and want to keep buying it? The player who can't turn a profit with A-H didn't pick investor so somebody buys it out from under you. Congrats! Playing without the investor card creates much more interesting timing puzzles.

RATING: 8
I have played mostly on the Ipad, as it's hard to get my group to commit to a 6 player game that goes several hours.  However, this is a winner.  The action rondel is unique and interesting; the light war gaming is simple but fun.  What makes this game so interesting is the bidding/country swapping.  You own bonds, not countries, so control of the board can change quickly.  You can build up a country's position only to see some other player swoop in and buy it out from you, as they profit from your hard work.  A lot of fun and only moderately "thinky" as people learn the bond system.  Once they get that down, it starts to flow quickly

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Despite the subjectively low score of "7" - I am very impressed by this game. I rarely rate games I don't own, but Imperial demands this courtesy. Best of the rondel series that I've played (all but Hamburgum), though solely due to the - initially off putting due to complexity - Taxation and Investment rondel options. I won't go into detail about how these two mechanisms operate, suffice to say  they demand a type of lateral thinking and player-piece disassociation not common in board gaming. Doubt it's truly original, nothing is, but very fresh to my experience. It's so unusual it seems to take at least 2-3 games for people to truly understand (I wouldn't say I was a master by a long shot, though I did win my last game).

Ideologically I'm very anti-capitalistic in my outlooks (i.e. bushy browed socialist), but one has to adopt the mentality of a robber baron to be really successful in this game. Ironically, I kind of like that. It takes a willful desire to exploit in order to win - unlike other economic games dressed in capitalist trappings (Power Grid, Caylus, etc). My score can only go up from here (2 plays in).

RATING: 8
While the board and pieces are strongly reminiscent of Diplomacy, Imperial is definitely not a game of running a country and conquering Europe.  It is a game of investment, with the countries best viewed as companies.  As in 1830, grow too attached to running a single company and you will probably lose.  The question you must always be asking is where your money would most profitably be placed.  I find it a very engaging game whose strategic subtleties will take several games to reveal themselves.

A couple of quibbles.  Imperial feels a more constrained, less free-wheeling game than either Diplomacy or 1830.  There is no dramatic dumping of shares, in fact there's no selling at all.  There is also no positive gain for a major power from invading another major power's territory, only the prospect of damaging production by neutralising a factory. As others have mentioned, the end game, with countries mostly racing from taxation to investment and back again, is anti-climatic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Wow!  After much research for a high-level-of-interaction game with depth, Imperial has taken my friends by storm.  I've been coming home from work to find my friends waiting and ready to play.  Imperial is by far the most popular game to hit our table since Settlers of Catan.

The premise of Imperial is that players are investors (bond holders) who can invest in combinations of the six countries present on the map.  The majority bond holder in a given country holds that countries flag and controls that countries action each turn.  So the turns are all country based.  Countries need to balance military production, expansion, taxation, and payout to bond holders.  The result is a game of shifting alliances and  country control requiring good tactics and meta-level thinking -- remember you are an investor, not a country.  The goals of each are often not perfectly aligned.

One other note about imperial is that it is not as fun until people learn some strategy.  Initially some players try to take one country to a military victory, once it is clear that does not work, the next game basically turned into an invest/tax/invest/tax/... race.  That was really boring.  Fortunately it turns out that the best players use a full mix of the games options, and that has turned our sessions into a delightful frenzy.

Length: 3 hours
Players: 3 - 6 all are very fun w/ decent players
Variants: I recommend the *advanced* initial bond buying once a few games have been played.  The author has also posted 'Imperial Negotiaion' varient (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/141857)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
With an early 19th century Europe setting, players represent international financial investors. What makes Imperial different is the Nations are completely separate from the players - or put in other words, players are stockholders and CEOs of the respective countries (read: companies). Control of the countries is not permanant, for he who holds the most bonds (read: principle shareholder) gets to direct the Nation's actions through an interesting mechanism called the "Rondel".

Do not be fooled: this is utterly an economic game. Satisfies my gamer's sweet spot: sufficiently meaty while not being too unnecessary long (less than 2.5 hours). Recommended for everyone who likes games with medium-high complexity but feels a bit put off by the German boardgames' (sometimes) too abstractness to take a try.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Hands down, the best investment game out there.

It's really great to see new players start managing the game and its strategy as if it was a game of "Risk" or "Diplomacy", and slowly realizing that the design is that of an INVESTMENT game and not that of a COMBAT game.

When you adapt your mindset to that, and realize that the success of your starting country should not be your top priority, it all comes together and the design reaches (for you) a whole new level of elegance and admiration.

For those that enjoy heavier economic games, this is a must try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
This is described as a Euro-ish 'war game' , but it is really just a majority influence game with an elimination element that masquerades as 'war'.  If the machines of war were left on the board and the majority force present claimed 'control', the game would play virtually the same.  The sole reason to remove the pieces is ensure enough space is available on the board to add new pieces,  So what are the players fighting for?  Money - specifically bond debts created by the ongoing 'war'.  The player that backs the strongest income potential country(s) in the end wins.  There are some surprisingly interesting decisions with the country moves on the rondel, but this simple mechanic is not enough to carry the game.  Overall, there is potential in the box, but it has a bit of an unrefined feeling. [GTI: AGSL]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
I enjoyed Antike, but this is better with the roundel well used.  First game I thought I was playing a war game until a third of the way through and I realised it was a stock market game.  The country that I had been possessive about at the beginning could be equally effectively developed by an opponent, and my investment still enhanced.

Further playings and the game gets better.  One of the few games I am happy to play with six.  My only quibble is that being fifth or sixth player can be a big disavantage if you have one opportunity less to invest (as this opportunity comes round so infrequently).  I note there are advanced rules that deal with this situation.  Alternativey, any player who has had fewer opportuinities to invest than the starting player should be given an extra opportunity to invest ay the end of the game.

RATING: 7
Eventhough it can handle 6, there is direct conflict involved.  Not a big fan of multiplayer wargames or derivatives of.  Diplomacy/negotiations are pretty significant in this game and I don't like it.  I don't like people yelling/cajoling to win, I find it to be tiresome very quickly.

First Game: 5-12-2007
Very interesting game.  It kinda feels like 18xx because you don't own anything other than money and stocks in countries, which is just a form of money.  However, unlike in 18xx, you cannot sell your stocks.  So you find yourself in a situation that a country is just sucking and there is no way for you to cut your loses.
We played the investor card and I don't like it.  A lot of the game hinges upon on what stocks you can buy at a very critical time and the investor card really hampers your options.  I guess it works for newbie games because it takes out a layer that they don't have to consider because they don't have the investor card.  I concede that there is strategy to it because you have to time your purchases and the use of the rondel to Investor action.  I would rather have the strategic flexibility of not being castrated by the Investor Card.  One other thing, I don't think this game plays at it's best with 6.  Too much downtime and not controlling a country, while viable, is boring.  Definitely, not going to purchase for my collection since I was only looking to buy it as a deep 6 player game.  I would rather buy an 18xx to get this kind of game.  However, it is still a cool game and I am willing to try it again but without the Investor Card.

RATING: 6.8
Well.
I disagree that this is his best game.
Though, this game is perfect for you if you like abstract wargames with heavy economic elements, like in 18XX. In my first play, I tried the "no flag" strategy, taking as many interest and investing frequently.
The game is a great design that mixes as perfect as possible the area control of abstract wargames and the commodity speculation of games with shares and investing.

I dislike area control, and did not enjoy this game much, in contrast with some other "more civilized" area control games that I prefer.

However I recommend it to anyone that likes the genre.

RATING: 4
Played once as a 5-player game without the Investor card.  It's Acquire/Diplomacy without any dice or randomness.  Although I like the concept of not owning a country, the "beginner" game makes it hard to actually invest in countries that you really want to invest in.  If your starting country gets hammered from other players making alliances, it's hard to generate enough capital in order to invest in those higher bonds!  I was fortunate to be in an alliance the entire game... but I could have easily have been the player hammered on. 
 
This game is probably more fun without the assignment of countries!  I would play again with the advanced rules in which players choose their own Bonds instead of just getting a starting country.  

Update:  Played a 2nd time as a 6-player game with the investor card, but also as the beginner game in which the starting countries were assigned.  I disliked how the game lost focus when one of the players lost his initial country.  He was shut out of the game for 90 minutes with nothing to do.  I love the theme and the gameplay is pretty good, but the potential for someone to be shut out for more than an hour is not something that I enjoy in gaming.

RATING: 9
Played this for the first time and won. Of course it was everybody else's first time too, so not that big of a deal. Strategy so far? Make friends, never skip an opportunity to make income or invest, pump up your factories early. Don't be afraid to take controlling interest in a nation. Use it to play one country off another for your own timely purposes.Phil has this.

You need (1)factories to (2)produce more units at one time and increase the value of the country. You can also (3)import up to 3 units for $1M each. You can (4)maneuver your armies and fleets 1 space each.

Choose (5)investor when you hold the most bonds for the country and when you have the investor card so you can get $2M and buy a bond.

Choose (6)Taxation to *get tax dividend (increase from last chart position) from the bank. *Add $2M per factory & $1M per chip - $1M per unit in money to the Nation coffer. *Increase the value of shares (power points) according to the blue number amount.

Rondel choice order:
Investor
Import
Production
Maneuver
Taxation
Factory
Production
Maneuver

Strategy: Factory, Production, Maneuver (take land or set up defense), Import units (gird position), Maneuver, Tax

RATING: 7.5
[Birthday present.]
This is Antike meets Acquire. I like that the countries take turns instead of the players. If you don't control any countries, you don't make decisions for them on their turns, but you still get to do your own stuff.

It always takes me about a third or half of the game to remember that I'm not the countries I control. This is not an area control game. It's all about investing. In my second game, I controlled only England for more than half the game, and it was a piddly little empire with not much to it, but even though the taxation amount didn't go up much or at all, and England hardly ever had money to pull out for the Investor action, it ended up winning me the game, because calling taxation over and over again still bumped up its multiplier for the end. And because it was so piddly for so long, one person didn't invest in it at all, and the other person didn't get to it till late.

This game can be difficult to get your head around, but it's totally worth it. Really, really fun. Played with 4 and with 3. Works great with both.

RATING: 8.1
Secret Santa 2007. Thanks a million!
Erroneously labelled as a Diplomacy variant in some circles, this game stands by itself as a "power behind the throne" epic. Yes, there's elements of Diplomacy gameplay here, but you could say it's more like Antike for stockbrokers. 

I like how the small value bonds give a better ROI than the larger ones - whereas the larger value bonds are needed for final scoring and to ensure that you can control a country (or more than one country, ideally).

Countries are just pawns in your mission for wealth, they can be stripped bare, wasted in "pointless" wars, whatever you like, as long as you turn a profit.

If someone is battering you with a certain country, buy in to it, take control of it, now you're safe!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Up to six players invest in, and vie for control of, the six major powers of 19th century Europe. The player who makes the shrewdest investments wins this game, irrespective of how "successful" any of these powers you may have controlled over the course of the game are by game's end.

Martin Wallace used a somewhat similar concept in his "Princes of the Rennaisance," but used the auction mechanic as the primary game engine. Here, Mac Gerdts uses a "rondel" (think a round dial movement spinner without the spinner) to regulate what happens in the game as the country markers progress around the dial.

The level of investment determines which player will make decisions for the respective powers. This harks back to "1830" where control can "change hands" as players leapfrog each other in level of investment. Unlike 1830, the investments are in terms of "bonds" rather than "stock certificates," so there is no up and down action of a stock market in this game, and, while you can upgrade a holding in a country, once invested you cannot divest yourself of the base investment. Hence, the decison to invest has long term consequences for your success. Guessing wrong can hurt!

This game looks and feels much like a traditional war-game until you play enough to realize that it is much more nuanced with the economic overlay that is incorporated. The number and type of investments is the real key and while battles between nations will occur, they are secondary to the object of having the best portfolio at game end. How best to accomplish this is not obvious and will having you coming back for more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Updated rating, now I've played over 10 times. Currently I like this and want to play it again - a Diplomacy type game that I actually like. Has a feel of 18xx in that you invest in the countries, run them and try to lift money out of them to win.

Oddity is you can't get out of a country - there's no mechanic to sell your bonds, but the payout is so high for each there's no real reason not too, and if the country doesn't have the cash then the owner has to pay instead [which means you don't put your country on the 'invest' space if the country hasn't got enough cash]

Scales well - with less players it feels a little more like a wargame. Trick is to remember you're in it for the money rather than playing as a government trying to win with your country. Lots of options, but I really don't like the basic (standard) game very much - advanced setup and playing without the investor card give a much more interesting game.

Artwork is good too - board helps game play, most borders are clear and areas big enough to fit the bits. Only change I've made is playing with poker chips rather than the paper money.

** Update, shine off this a bit - some nations are just better than others with the static setup and contraints of the map. 2030 version is an improvement. Rating knocked down a peg.

RATING: 5
I've only got one play so I'll probably hold off reviewing it for a while; however, this game strikes me as a great resource/economic/interactive game.  The basic premise is that players are investing in one or more of 6 countries in pre-WWI Europe.  As the countries become more economically strong, they go up on the victory track (even if they weaken later).  At end game, you multiply your stake in each country by their place on the victory track.  So you get points for diversifying while making sure those countries you are the most invested in are doing better than the others.  Therefore, there are never any inherent enemies -- you can pair up with any player if you are both investing in the same country which makes for interesting alliances.  Overall, I really enjoyed this game and am looking forward to playing again.

RATING: 8
Imperial is an awesome game.  In many ways, it is like Chicago Express.  It's a bit less fun (and longer) than CE, but much deeper.  While we've begun to get a feel for how to play CE well after 6 plays, with Imperial we are just beginning to sense what playing well might mean - still haven't seen it for sure, and can't imagine it will click for many more games.  The balances and tipping points share-wise are much harder to figure in Imperial, with the early game feeling very open (almost to the point of flailing around) and the endgame coming on quick and strong, so much so that you might lose out majorly if you don't time your purchases and movements well.

Another plus for Imperial is simplicity.  Like CE, this is an amazingly simple game to learn and play, but the depth built into these few rules is truly inspiring.  The rondel is a real innovation, and makes for many tough decisions and tasty possibilities.  While I rated this one as highly as CE, it probably won't make it into my top 10, just because it is significantly harder to figure.  That said, I can imagine the day where the tables are turned, and we've gotten a bit tired of CE, whereas I can't imagine Imperial not always rewarding.

RATING: 10
[u]July 2008:[/u] The game is tense, very tense. Each time you buy a bond, you'll be wondering if it is the right choice. There are lots of strategies that work. You must adapt to how the game flows and it always flows differently. I've played 15 times on BSW and watched more than 60 other games and I've seen different stuff each time.
This game is a must and is largely underrated on BGG IMHO. It needs a little time to get used to the system as it really is totally counter intuitive.
The Game is great with 3 or 4 players. 5 players is ok but it gets crowded, 6 is too much for my taste and 2 player game just fails to grasp the beautiful intricacies the game provides.

[u]May 2009: [/u]
Just to add, don't buy this thinking it's a confrontation game. This is a manoeuvering game. You are not fighting one country against another, here, you are tampering with countries. Get rid of the idea that one country belongs to you. The reason why I love this game is for the macchiavelean aspect. It could be a very good idea to attack one of your country with another one of your countries!
Here, your goal is not to bring your country to splendour, but to win money!!!

I just can't say how much I love this game, this is my only 10.

RATING: 8.5
Modestly prone to groupthink even with good players (even more so with inexperienced players).  Faintly reminiscent of the 1829 branch of 18XX crossed with Acquire. Surprisingly emergently complex for such a simple system.  Don't bother with the beginner setup with pre-allocated shares.  Also don't bother with the investor card.  Go straight to the advanced variant without the investor card.  It is far more interesting.  Also, bin the paper money and use poker chips for faster and easier play.  

Note the rhythmic power of countries on opposite sides of the board from each other.  There are interesting phase systems that can be worked with such country pairs.

When played without the investor card this rates an easy 8.5 for me.  When played with the investor card it falls to a dismal 3.  It simply isn't worth playing with the investor card.

The game without the investor card is notably phase-driven and moderately money starved.  The reduced economy makes the exact timing of bond purchases far more important to specific country fortunes and to final player net worth than when playing with the investor card (each bond purchase forms a larger percentage of the   player's final holdings as less bonds are bought). 

The game is far improved by playing with a group dedicated to Imperial.  There's an easy whole point rating available for that special circumstance.  Sadly that's not locally available, not in the manner I prefer, so Imperial is shelved out of rotation and sits at 8.5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
UPDATE 12/8/17: Finally after many moons got in (part of) a game without the investor card and with the advanced startup rules. Advanced start I would recommend for anyone who's played more than a couple of times, though drawing lots for starting bonds still seems like a strong choice as an introduction.

Playing without the investor card [i]does[/i] improve the game, though it does apply a slight brake to the snappy pace of the game that is part of what makes this so good, and I cordially disagree with people who regard the game with the investor card as trash.

:sugar: :( :what: :what: :D :) :) The anti-war war game, basically [i]War Is A Racket[/i] rendered in chipboard and hardwood. Players of Imperial are financiers who buy bonds in the six great powers of Europe pre-WW1. (Sorry, Turkey.) You can buy stock (bonds, technically) in any or all of the countries, and their value at the end of the game is a direct result of both their success at military and/or industrial expansion and the frequency with which they tax those assets. A la 18xx/Chicago Express, countries have their own treasuries with a strict distinction between them and their controller's personal treasuries. Also like those games, national (corporate) treasuries are dead money in terms of win conditions, so one partial path to victory is contriving ways to slough those treasuries back into your personal pocket.

Also like 18xx, the game is totally deterministic, with no elements of chance save the initial draw of bonds (in the standard rules) or the person who starts the auction (in the advanced rules).

The rondel mechanism is the clever bit, because it forces a tough choice on each country's controller (the player with the highest total bond holdings in that country): go slowly around the rondel and you are better positioned to build factories and/or build military and maneuver them into neutral spaces that improve your tax position, but this same slow roll around the rondel means you less frequently hit the taxation (pumps money into national treasury and gives the country the power points that make their bonds more valuable at game's end) and investor (disburses national treasury money to those greedhead bondholders and enables new bond purchases) spaces.

First-timers frequently get tripped up by assuming that a board that looks like Diplomacy implies a game that plays like Diplomacy, get over-attached to their one country, and end up overvaluing conquering the map. But it gets trickier, because it's easy to swing to the opposite extreme and overrate portfolio-diversification vs. picking a winner and making them win.

One recommendation, especially for learning games: at game's end, instead of calculating final scores on paper, buy back bonds for money equal to bond value in points x the credit rating, most money wins. It's totally equivalent but it hammers home the point that money is also points and the purchase of an expensive bond in a low-performing country (say, one at Credit x 1) is actually less good in the final analysis than just keeping the money -- those 8 and 9 level bonds are only worth the effort after a few spins between Investor and Taxation on the rondel.

If the mark of a good game is the ability to play many times and not feel like you've come close to really learning its tricks, Imperial is quite a good game indeed.

RATING: 6
After one play, I don't feel qualified to give an in depth review, but I wanted to record my first impressions. The game is well designed. The mechanics were unique to me and interesting. I had difficulty identifying strong stategic goals, but I also didn't have a clear grasp on the interactions between the rules. I didn't even realize that the leader of a country got bonus pay during a good tax season. Clearly, there is a benefit to controlling a country, expanding, and taxing. The bonus pay for being the leader of a country whose taxes increase seems important. Also, owning a controlling interest in the lead country is important. But it is difficult to buy bonds in the lead country without exposing yourself and losing control of the countries you currently control. It sucks waiting through a turn when you don't control a country.

I'd definitely play again, but I was not enamored my first time through like I was with Agricola.

RATING: 8
It feels a bit like 18XX except that once you own stock (bonds) in a country you can never get rid of them. The "ownership" or control of the government, rather, does change hands some...but if you gut a country you'll likely still be the owner. 

There is a great balance of tensions on a few different levels. For example, simply the balance between having a country perform well and benefiting you personally by paying out. 

I feel like I need to put more thought into how to play well...its certainly not intuitive, nor is it static. You can't enter a game with a set strategy without adapting to the events of the game, or you'll lose. The learning curve is for strategy only, as the rules are quite straightforward. 

In my first game game, immediately after playing one player ordered the game and another made a trade to obtain a copy. And all 4 of us wanted to play again right away. I look forward to getting more satisfaction from this one.

I'd rank this one a bit higher than Antike, if I could only choose one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Imperial is the stock market based version of the Eggertspiele Rondel system which is also present in Antike and Hamburgum.

Here there are 6 countries vying for control of Europe. The catch? They each have a portfolio of stocks available, and one player can control several countries, one country, or no countries. The goal is to make money, and the way to do that is to simply invest in the countries which are making the soundest financial decisions. There is a combat engine present, which can be either very important, or very un-important given the mindset of your group. Suffice to say that having a potential defence is always good, but avoiding combat, in general, is a good thing, because of opportunity costs.

The system here is fairly opaque, which I find appealing. Players have to juggle several balls at once to make a profit. Money goes into country coffers, which then muster armies and take over territory so they can earn more money, which can then be moved back out into player hands.

Control of the right stocks at the right time is crucial. Which means that I must bring up the Investor card / no Investor card thing. I have played the game using each variant, and I think it works with either. The downside to the no Investor card is the lack of money. The downside to the card is the potential advantage to the early turn players, as they will likely have more opportunities to invest (assuming the late turn players don't catch up by spending some time flag-less just for that purpose). I think I would like to play with the combined rules, where the Investor card is circulating, but only hands out money, while players are still allowed to invest in each country after it takes its turn ala the no card rules.

The main downside with this game is that I feel it takes a bit long for what it is. In the same time frame I could easily play a Prairie Rails game. In a longer time frame, I could pull out an 18XX game. I would enjoy either of those options more, because they strip away the war game veneer and just let players go at it in the pure economic arena, which is really where this game's action and heart is at anyway. This is the kind of game that I pulled out as many times as I did simply because I really like the premise, but the game just isn't quite there for me. The flow of the game is simply too slow in terms of power point build-up, and every time I play it it seems to wear out its welcome beyond the second hour. In theory, stock games can work without sales but I think they need to be something very special, and I definitely think Imperial would have benefited from some sort of market, as a side benefit it would have made the theme fit the game more appropriately.

All of this said, I think this is an interesting system, and I still wouldn't mind playing it every so often because it does do a few cool things, but I still feel like this game system would be much better if it had been refined in some way to make it less of an utter slog to build up power points.

RATING: 4
My first experience with Imperial was... how to say this... not good. First of all, the explanation was horrendous, leaving me none the wiser what I was supposed to be doing and how to go about getting it done. This will kill any game, so is not really Imperial's fault. But the game had me thinking of Diplomacy with stock markets with Antike's rondel mechanic (which is clever, but which I'm not overly fond of), and I made the decision to leave my stock up for grabs after about 20 minutes. I really have to try again when I know a bit more what I'm supposed to be doing—perhaps then it will be better.

[b]UPDATE[/b] And so the second opportunity came to pass. I understood what the game was about much better, although there is a wide gulf between such understanding and playing well enough to win, of course. As with all games involving stocks or bonds (in Imperial it's bonds) you want others to finance your expansion elsewhere. Those others can be the other players or the bank. Other players invest in a country, and that money will flow into your pockets through dividends; the bank generates taxes which also end up in your pockets as dividend, and you get a small bonus paid by the bank when a country's wealth goes up. Even if you don't take control of a country, it pays off to have bonds all over the place simply because it increases your liquid assets and thus purchasing power. If for whatever reason you cannot get this feedback loop up and running well enough, you lose the game. Plain and simple.

Imperial's stock manipulation mechanisms are polished and allow plenty of exploration, so no problems there. But it's the execution which sours me on this title. One issue I have with this game is that blasted rondel again. If you extend yourself using as little military force as possible (or extend yourself by burning off the soldiers), you want to first tax the country (so the obligations go up in value), and then immediately play investor (so you get dividend you can use to invest elsewhere). But in between those two steps are five other countries, and by the time you can take the second action, someone else may have grabbed the investor card—and doing that action without you holding the card is not such a good idea. The rondel breaks up a core mechanic of the game into uneven steps which [i]might[/i] make for an interesting journey, but I'm not one for such finesses. The second issue is the awful 1:1-combat rule. As with Antike this heavily favours the defender. It takes time and effort to build up a force capable of reigning in others, and every time you take another step to work on this aspect, someone else might be going for the quick cash with which he can simply out-finance you. Or the military situation simply changes for the worse. So the game tends to devolve into trench warfare with stagnant nations: powerful ones stayed powerful, weak ones stayed weak. A player controlling bonds in a strong nation has no real incentive to weaken that country's position, at least not for a while. (He might in order to ruin a country and then jumping into another country with the 'bail out money', so to speak. If the players controlling bonds in that nation don't see this coming, they have a major problem. However, such a manoeuver is tricky and won't occur regularly.) A player controlling bonds in a weak nation has no clout to hit the others with. This gives Imperial a rather tacky feel. The final issue is a corollary of the above: players should prevent such a situation at all cost, but since it is a deterministic design, player chaos reigns supreme. Wheeee...

As I said, the stock manipulation is challenging, I'll give Imperial that. The marriage to the rondel more or less sinks the game for me. Without the rondel and a more fluid attack model the game would be right up my alley. Basically, control in this game is very fleeting. All you can do is create incentive, and hope that others do a bit of a legwork too. I tend not to like such games very much: I am the one playing, so I want to do something, not hide in the coulisses all the time. I might play it again in the future, but I'm not holding my breath, to be honest.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
A stock market game in which the purchase price of the shares is unrelated to their value, and you only get the option to purchase shares at specific times, so may be locked out of the game for considerable periods. The market is based on the movement of the values of countries, which can only go up, but at variable rates - huge leaps in country value in the endgame and the inability to sell stock, make rational investing very difficult. The meta-wargame that moves country values up and down is quite amusing but very simplistic and again, since only lead share-holders run countries, you may be locked out of playing a country for considerable periods but still win. Dreary, irrational, long.
Nov 2012.  I tried Imperial 2030. The board is more interesting than the original, but all of the weaknesses noted above remain. Interestingly, I played with experienced players and the winner was a complete surprise to everyone, until points were counted.

RATING: 1
Imperial is a very very strange beast. Instead of controlling 1 specific country, you are an evil war profiteer, investing in the great powers of WW1. As a new player, it's very difficult to understand what's a good move, as comtrol of these countries shifts about.

You really only take actions when you have control of a country, meaning there can be long periods of time where you don't do anything. When I play a game I expect for it to be 2 things: fun & engaging. This game fundamentally fails to be engaging, as you can sometimes get up and leave for 20 minutes and would not have missed any of your actions actions. Couple that with a fairly long length (3 hrs) and you get disinterested players napping on the couch.

Is there anything good about the game? Yes. The theme is fantastic, and by far the best part. Acting as an evil top hat & monacle wearing war profiteer is hilarious. Pulling the strings behind the countries is a fun idea.

Imperial is a great idea hampered by boring gameplay.

RATING: 7
First play: Favorably impressed. Of the three Euro-AT hybrids I have played in the last year, this is by far my most positive experience (my other two plays? Tempus and Shogun). Takes a bit of getting used to the idea that you are primarily playing the role of an investor, rather than a political/military leader. First play with five people, none of whom had ever played the game, took 4 hours. We played with NO investor card and ADVANCED rules, and I anticipate that our group will opt to play this way always. All players recognized early on that this is NOT Risk, and that the goal was NOT to conquer the board. After one play, there are naturally a lot of tactical optimizations and strategies to be worked out yet, which is a good thing - the game is interesting enough to think about after a play, leaving the players looking forward to their next play. Components are of high quality, if not spectacular, and the rules are fairly clean - we had no questions which we could not answer by searching the rule booklet for a minute or two.

RATING: 8
As the nations of Europe battle it out in a very simple wargame, players "invest in bonds" in the different countries to both control the action and hopefully end up backing the right horses.  The combination of a simple wargame and a simple stock market (well, sorta) game makes for a real brain-burner and real fun.  Takes a play or two to really wrap your head around it, but it's worth the effort.  The only negative is a potential downtime issue when a player doesn't control a majority in any country (which allows you to make its moves on the map) and doesn't get a chance to invest for a while.

RATING: 8
An interesting blend of Diplomacy (land and naval units, diceless combat) and 18xx games (shares, payments, payouts). Except you've got factories that tie into your economy, the whole Rondel mechanism, and you're usually not playing any one particular nation for long.

I continue to enjoy this each time I play it. I haven't played the Basic Game vs the Advanced Game enough to decide which I prefer yet, however.

On a side note, the rulebook for this version is quite terrible. Note the 3-page FAQ for a game that really shouldn't be that difficult. Thankfully, the FAQ is quite helpful. The rulebook for Imperial 2030 is much better.

RATING: 8
This is really great if you think of it as Acquire with corporate raiders instead of cannons.  But the actual warfare element kinda weighs it down.  If everybody plays aggressively, every country will be broke, and the game will never end.  If Taxation was in some way automatic so the game had to proceed to an ending, it would be a 9.

Oh wait, 8 instead of 7.  If there were only 4 players, then 2 would have two countries and 2 could team up.  Then what you are working for is securing an alliance that allows you to concentrate on expanding and keep a peaceful front.  Then you might not be stuck in an infinite attrition battle.  I can dig it.  Need to play this a bunch more with fewer players.

Must be played without the investor card.  The investor card puts too much luck in the table order (some people will have more chances to buy stock in the game) and gives WAY too much power to a person with no countries.  He can buy a 1 or 2 stock every turn, which is much more efficient for getting VPs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players represent the power behind the politics during the 19th century.  Players "invest" in countries, purchasing bonds which generates cash flow for those countries.  The largest investor in each country gets to play the turn for that country, allowing them to tax, pay off bonds, build up armies and fleets, move those armies/fleets, etc.  The object of the game is to make the most money, and most of it is made by your bonds which escalate in value based on how well the country did in terms of conquest.  Lots of good stuff here, reminiscent of Antike (the Rondel is back, so everything is quick and pacy).  Very good game.

RATING: 5.5
Imperial looks at first glance like a dudes-on-a-map territorial control game, but that's deceptive.  It's really a stock market, economic, investment style game.  And that's not really my type of game.  
I will say, though, that this one is much more approachable than others (*cough* 18XX *cough*), since you can see the country's power growing on the board as it gobbles up more territory, and see its progression along the stock value/power track.  It won't be obvious at first, since they're all evenly matched, but one will start to pull ahead, and you'll have to decide whether to jump on the bandwagon and invest in it, or try and use its neighbors to reel it in a bit.  The rondel mechanic also works here, as there's kind of a cycle to what you're trying to do.  Expand, use the new territory to make money, invest some of that cash in future growth, use that growth to make more troops, and expand again.  Somewhere in there is the most important action, investing, where the company pays out funds from its treasury to the shareholders (so you get your cut of the proceeds).  
So, for a boring economic game, it's not bad.  Hard to teach to new players though (always someone who wants to play it like risk), and can take quite a while.

RATING: 10
This game presents a wonderfully fresh perspective on the global/political domination genre.  At first glance it would appear to be a basic territorial conquest game, but in reality, it's all about monetary investments and profit.  The military forces in the game are just tools to be used to increase wealth and power.  What makes it especially interesting is that control of nations can change during the course of the game and multiple players can simultaneously have a stake in the same nation's fate.  Imperial is a beautifuly executed game design that keeps players involved throughout.

Advanced setup and the variant with no Investor card are definitely the best way to play.

RATING: 2
There are several major problems with this game:

1. The normal method of buying shares is dreadful.

2. Once a player or/and a country is doing well there is a snowball effect where the struggling players just fall further and further behind.

3. Your fate is determined far more by what everyone does than you do, which doesn't work in such a "heavy" game.

4. At the end of the game, even if you get trounced, you can't see anywhere where you went drastically wrong or what you could have done to improve.

RATING: 4
[2 plays] This isn't a bad game, it's just not my cup of tea.  Stock market plus war = I'm bored just thinking about it.  So I was pleasantly surprised after the first play when it wasn't that dreadful.  But the second time sort of clarified what else I don't like about it.  The game ends sooner than you might think it will the first time; it ends when any country gets to the end of the power track, and once they've become decently powerful, they can leap way ahead down this track in a single turn.  Once people realize that the game will end soon, it sort of enters this tedious endgame phase where nothing you can do is much use, and everyone is intensely calculating the possible outcomes.  

Anything you actually do on the ground takes time.  Producing units is one turn, and sending them anywhere requires another turn.  And outside one's own nation they can only move one space at a time, so it could take a good while just for your fleet or army to get to where the action is. Basically everything takes time to come to fruition.  Once there isn't that much time left, then actually DOING anything becomes pointless, and all that's left is financial maneuvering. 

And that amounts to a bunch of chess-like thinking (If I do this, then they might do that, and then I would want to do this, etc.), but where every possibility is also a cumbersome mathematical calculation.  It kind of felt like at a certain point we stopped playing the actual game and then spent 30 minutes figuring out who won.  Ho hum.

RATING: 9
After one game with three and the investor card, it's gone in my pantheon of games that I stand in the shower thinking about the day after I played for the first time.  I expect that the rating may improve with more plays, as the deep analysts on BGG have given me confidence that the replayability is there.  Unlike with Antike, I like the deterministic combat here (and the application of the rondel is also better), since the fluidity of nation control means that battle is merely a sideshow to the skullduggery underneath and so there's no reason to draw it out.

A pretty remarkable and elegant design - to make something so easy to learn, without cubes, cards, or counters.  It has a thematic feel, appears to scale well between 3 and 5, and provided a deep experience in just enough time to fit into a weeknight session.  What more can you want?  I guess my one concern is the anticlimactic end game.

RATING: 6
I genuinely think this game is flawed. Essentially, the Credit Value of shares in a nation rise even when the nation's empire collapses. This is totally contrary to any normal economic model. It pays to invest in a failing state, on the basis that the last taxation will push its Credit highest. Surely, the Credit Value is the Earnings per Share? Why would that rise when the value of the share has fallen. That crashing flaw aside, this is a very different game. The rondel as a mechanism did not interest me much, the fixed cycle of the nations' turns did not seem valid (why would Austria wait until russia had acted, and why would Austria always get an action before Italy??), but the separation of the players from the sates and the shifting of control by the share mechanism was interesting. The game seemed to end much too quickly for my liking, and there was too much book-keeping and general fiddliness. And I lost with half the score of the winning player.

RATING: 10
What a delicate balance of opposing forces.  Every time you buy a bond you have to weigh the relative benefits of a tax bonus, player motivation, and bond payouts at the end and in the near future.  Add to this the decisions that take place on the board with respect to your own personal cash and the struggle for more taxes and you've truly got an exceptional game.  

The really great thing about Imperial is that I think it's approachable.  There's few people that didn't love it the first time they played, and the others eventually followed suit.

I've heard the investor card variants described as good but different games, but I feel like they're both the same.  The investor card just introduces a bit more leeway for beginners at the expense of control.  I vastly prefer the no-investor card variant now, but I'd never turn down a game with it.

RATING: 7.5
O jogo se passa na Europa. Os jogadores são investidores que adquirem ações dos países. Não é bom se apegar aos países pois eles mudam de mão durante a partida. Do mesmo autor do Antike. Também tem a Rondel. As decisões não envolvem sorte. Somente as ações escolhida pelos jogadores para cada país que domina. Você pode construir fábricas, produzir unidades terrestres e marítimas, movimentar, investir, importar unidades e fazer taxação. Ele se passa por um jogo de guerra. Tem unidades, conflitos, territórios mas na realidade é um jogo econômico pois comandará o país ou os país o jogador que tiver mais ações daquele país. E no fim do jogo vence quem tem mais dinheiro. Bem interessante. Relativamente pesado e demorado mas vale o tempo.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Imperial manages to elegantly merge the two game categories that I most like: economics and war. 

The game appears at first sight to be mostly a war game and some players will tend to play it that way, but for me the economical (stock market) side is the most fascinating. There are truly two games being played in parallel, where war/country development influences the stock market and the contrary is even more noticeable. As country stocks can be bought by several players, partial alliances are quite common - a player may have an alliance with another for a specific country, but be in war for another country. This creates a nice platform for complex player interactions which can create tremendous fun and chaos.

Imperial asks for players which mostly favour strategy and great player interaction, but prefer simple and elegant gameplay without excessive rules/details.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
2016
====

Imperial is Mac Gerdts' masterpiece. On the surface the game looks a bit like Risk. Heck, on my first play I played it like Risk and was utterly humiliated. The reason for my total defeat was that the game only uses direct conflict as a means to an end -- bond valuation. That is, the point of the game is gaining control of the production, taxation, and war efforts of countries by purchasing bonds and then using that power to drive up the value of said bonds. Perhaps war will help in doing so or perhaps not it's so hard to say for certain -- determining so is entirely situational. The point is that the game is not a war game per se, but one of subtle manipulations, fleeting alliances, bond diversification, and only occasionally war. Imperial is a game of "shadow governments" and oligarchies -- it's quit nihilistic to be quite honest -- but its nature makes for something quite special.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good game! Stealing control of a powerful nation to your enemy is very satifying!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I only don't give this a 10, because I don't have enough plays under my belt and can not know if it will pass the test of time. (Played it about 10 times so far.)
The rules are about as simple as they can be, but they build up a very rich game. There is no extra luck, and no hidden information, but all the stuff happening on the board keeps it from getting dry. Trying to find and manipulate the patterns in the chaos is a very hard and entertaining task. Its chaotic nature also keeps most players from overanalyzing things. Still, one needs to pay attention to almost every country, so it's even engaging when it's not your turn. The one complaint that I can come up with is that I find the artwork ugly.
The exceptionally elegant and innovative design plus the high player-interaction makes this a favourite of mine.

Update: it stays at 9. The beginning and the ending plays very similarly in each game, which is a bit dull.

RATING: 8
Shows how good a euro can be when there is tons of interaction.   The central mechanic of owning stock and controlling countries and the fighting over territory, battles, etc leads to all the interaction.  You might control a country one minute and be fighting against it in just a few turns.  The math can bother you a little and the game could benefit from unpredictability in combat, IMO, but I'm probably in a very small minority of imperial players on those issues.  The only real concern with the game is some kingmaking.  Its a top 10 game in my opinion and I think with time its BGG rating will show that.  

This is probably my favorite euro-style game.  

Update:  Many plays later I still really like this game, but I no longer feel it is a world beater destined for the top 10.  I dropped the rating down because the game strongly depends on the players playing sheriff and watching each other for it's balance.  In a way it is unfair to knock a game for this.  Any game which is significantly interactive and uses a VP scoring system is going to have this issue.  Problem is, after playing a lot of games of imperial, if you aren't all watching each other like hawks someone always seems to sneak one by.  Part of that is why I like it so much, but I now recognize that it is a flaw in its own way too.  Still one of my favorite games.

RATING: 6
Rating after one play.  Cool idea for a game, unique system... still not sure how well it fits together.

We played with the investor card, which seemed to really limit the amount of investing you could do.  A lot of "investments" were actually purchased toward the end of the game when their value was fairly secure.  We played with 5 players and the final scores ranged from 91 to 110, which felt almost artificially close.  The winner actually lost control of government about halfway through the game and then was able to build a very diversified portfolio while the rest of us struggled for the opportunity to buy any bonds at all.  It seemed wrong that a player should be so heavily rewarded for something (not having any flags) they had little to no control over.  I had one flag at the time and a fair amount of cash and would've given anything to lose my flag and be in his position.

The end game was pretty unsatisfying.  The return value of the each available bond was easily calculated, and there seemed to be no interesting choices left but to buy the bond with the best (immediate) return on investment.  It also seems like there should be some risk involved in the bond market (for example if a country completely collapses or is occupied by enough enemy forces, the bonds wouldn't pay at the end of the game)

RATING: 7
An interesting take on ANTIKE, but more complex.  The action "wheel" is still present, and works similarly, although there is one spot which triggers the Investment sub-phase whenever a Country Action Token moves on or over it!

Each player will be playing two roles during the game.  That of an Investor, purchasing Bonds from the various Countries, and that of a Government, deciding when to tax, produce units, move/attack as well as when to pay dividends.

Managing both of these can be difficult, as players may purchase Bonds on any Country, and the player with the most Bonds (total) forms the new Government (i.e. controls that Country).

Initially players may have difficulties as to where the money is placed.  When a Country plays the Tax action, the money generated, if any, gets added to that Country's treasury.  When the Country pays it's inventors, the money is taken from the Country's Treasury and paid to the players holding Bonds.

So you'll be spending your money investing in different Countries, which you can be certain will change hands more than once during the game.  In the end, it is the player with the most money (cash on hand   value of all Bonds) who wins.

Although I rated this a '7', I think my rating will go up after a few more plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Imperial is likely the best rondel game Gerdts has created, and possibly the best game overall period.

The game play is as straightforward as other rondel games, but the consequences of those choices- especially around the investor action- can be major, and involve other players in an opportunity to change the status quo.

To my knowledge, none of the other rondel games do this- with the possible exception of military actions in Antike- and none play with the pieces quite so much, and yet affect perhaps less than they seem.

The goals of the game is to finish with the most cash.  To do this, players optimize national bonds issued by pre-WWI European nations, in order to control the countries' actions, in order to build up their Tax and Power values, and in order to rake cash dividends from the nations.

Some would say it is Diplomacy (setting) meets 18XX (stock owenership)- but because of the rondel making actions less repeatable it has it's own flavour, and the actions take less hard fiscal calculation than an 18XX.

I like it a lot- glad I picked up a copy- and expect that eventually this will surpass previous Gerdt favorites Concordia and Navegador.

This is a great game with simple mechanics that aren't simple, but which also do not induce major AP.

The wooden bits are classic, but very cool- a great purchase, I predict.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
great economic game that puts you on the wrong foot, if you've ever played Diplomacy. use the rules for the investor from Imperial 2030 and it is better.

RATING: 8
What happens when Antike and 18XX slam into each other? You get Imperial. The simple action rondel mechanism, and simple movement and combat system, combine with the stock market complexities from 18XX. No single player "owns" a country, except as indicated by the number of shares he has invested in that country. The players' money are separate from the countries' money, like in 18XX or Chicago Express. The key is how to get the money in the countries' treasuries back into one's own pocket, so that he can buy more shares of a country that is doing well, or buy a controlling stake in another country, previously "owned" and "run" by another player. The game is much slower, in terms of physical movement, since the emphasis is on economics and not conquest; it is near impossible to take over another country, and it is something you probably don't want to--it is simply too expensive and takes too long to accomplish. It plays tactically a lot like Diplomacy, as it has a similar board and layout, but production plays heavily into conquering new lands instead of negotiating support from your allies. Diversification may do well, if you are able to buy shares in several countries that are doing well on the VP track, but getting money to buy those shares is difficult if you've made some poor choices early on, or your country isn't doing so well. Fascinating dichotomy between investing and expansion. I prefer Antike for its purity, and straightforward fast play, but would rather play this instead of an 18XX game if I want a stock market game, with a touch of war, all in about two hours.

RATING: 7
If you can accept the rather odd premise of being a shareholder in the imperial powers of Europe, then this is a reasonable maximise-your-holding game.  The map looks almost uncannily like a Diplomacy board, but it's badly missing the presence of Turkey. This gives Austria and Russia a free run at the Balkans while it's far less likely that Britain (for instance) will be an expansionist power, which makes some countries a much more attractive investment than others. Unlike 18xx, shares have no current value, so you can't sell any duds that you pick up near the start. Some multiplayer chaos in which share tiles are available and as a portfolio of small tiles has a better return than a single large one, this is a factor in who wins. The 2030 sequel has a more open board, but includes the same issues.

RATING: 7
Don't be confused by the box: This is really not a 6 player game. All countries starting with a dominant shareholder tends to mean players just strengthen their holding in their start country, and thus no countries can change hands. The game is therefore reduced to a dull wargame.

With 4 or 5 (ideally 4) the game is much more interesting, as the shares and economics become the focus of the game. Invest in the right countries, and extract as much cash as you can from those you control, while still trying to get them a good end-game bonus.

Imperial 2030 has a few small improvements that make it the better game, but plain Imperial isn't too shabby either.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I currently (fall 2014) have a moderately intense infatuation with Imperial.  Imperial is essentially a stock market game with a delightful twist: the players are international bankers who invest in the great powers of Europe before the dawn of World War I.  Essentially, the game opens withe the players holding varying levels of investment in the different nations.  The player holding the greatest bond value in each nation controls the actions of a particular nation.  Control of nations will pass from one player to another several times during the game when the new owner lends a large sum of funds to the nation, thus making him the player with the greatest level of investment.  The player who has accumulated the greatest personal wealth, which is separate from the nations' treasuries, when the game ends is the winner.   The game looks like a war game; there are naval units and armies that do combat.  But if you try to play this game like a military conquest game, you are doing it wrong and will likely lose badly.  I love Mac Gerdts' series of games that use the rondel for action selection.  The rondel dramatically reduces down time between turns.  Games with the rondel have a strong tendency to promote a pleasantly flowing game rhythm.  Imperial is radically different from any game I've ever played, so I find it very fascinating.  Imperial is like some kind of bastard child whose parents are a modern economic Euro game and and oldschool light conquest game.  There is no chance in the game at all--no dice or cards.  The fun and challenging part to Imperial is being wise in which country to invest in.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

RATING: 6.5
This is actually a solid game which I have a problem to master.

Innovative idea of struggle for control over the countries and changing 'ownership' of them.  Nice balance between military operations and actions increasing the power/wealth of countries players control.

There are 2 problems:
- the game is a bit too long (~3h with just 4 players)
- the game is _NOT_ for 5-6 players
[The game is UN-PLAY-ABLE with 6 players (no control of a country by 1-2 player(s) for a _long_ time).  It's very long (4h) and tiresome too...

With 5 it's better, but still there might be periods when a player does not control a country - these periods are however shorter than with 6p. and bearable in some cases.

Not controlling a country means not only boredom during a game, but most importantly no chance of winnning (most frequently), because of Taxation, which gives payouts only to players with control.  And snowball effect occurs - controllers get richer while non-controllers don't - and they are often powerless to do anything.]

The sweet spot is 4 players.  And it might be fine with 3 too, but I have to try it first...

EDIT: After 8 plays I'm full.  I feel I've explored the game enough.  I don't have a desire for repeated plays.  At least not so often to justify possessing a copy.

Sold.

RATING: 10
Great economic game of shifting alliances that has a very different experience depending on who you play with.  It can be very aggressive and warlike or much more cooperative or (usually) a nice mix of both.  By far the best of the 3 rondel games and a game that I am always up for playing.  Play with poker chips and open money though.  You can play on BSW as well but you lose some of the interesting player interaction that goes on (in my games as least).  I can see how this would be a little less fun with groups that don't have much table talk but I love the diplomacy aspect of the game -- "you really need to attack Germany because he is clearly winning" or "I'm obviously doing a great job running Russia for us so you should buy France instead".  This is a very interactive game and definitely not another multiplayer solitaire like some of the current hot games (I'm looking at you Agricola and Dominion).

2011-09-19

Initial Rating: 5.5 (September 2011)

I had such high hopes for Imperial..... it is as of today (19/09/2011) number 37 in the ranking here on BGG, I think it is a good to regular game but not a great one. I find Imperial to be very sluggish so I was bored between turns. I really like the idea of the game, its zero luck factor and the stock manipulation required to win.... but the execution of those ideas leave much to be desired (you can win by doing almost nothing as other players can run the countries for you leaving you with little to do). Also the manual is ambiguous in a lot of situations and the game can take a lot of time.  

You can tell it is from the same guy that designed Antike (which I like more) but the Rondel mechanic is beginning to bother me, I feel it makes its games slow.

In conclusion Imperial has some nice ideas but it fails to excite me. I’m glad I didn’t buy it.

RATING: 8.5

RATING: 9
Disentangling players from specific countries seems obvious in retrospect but was genius.  I will never turn this game down.  It is an experience comparable to an 18XX game in a tight 2-hour package, with pre-war Europe as the playing ground.

All player numbers from 3-6 work, but in different ways.  3-player is a ridiculously volatile good time -- though you can hide out and control no countries if you really work at it.  4-player is a good "base" setup.  5 and 6 play more like investment games, and I think introduce enough complexity in the incentive structures to discourage really analytical play; I have to play a 5-6 player game by feel.

With the investor card I don't like that the initial Austria controller has fewer investment opportunities - only a problem with the basic setup but I've been in a couple teaching games recently.

Without the investor card - so much more control!  A somewhat different playstyle is required though, as one can't rely on the free payouts anymore.  I don't know if I'd recommend playing without the investor card to inexperienced players but with an experienced group it's definitely the way to go.

I'm on the brink of just declaring I won't play with the investor card anymore.  I've had more than one frustrating game recently where I control Austria and get hamstrung by a lack of investment opportunities.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wonderful, wonderful game.

It carries forward the best bits of the designer's earlier Antike (the rondel and the way it splits the play down into small chunks, of which there never seem to be enough to do everything you want) while improving on the context. 

I find Antike's weaker elements to be the card collection mechanisms used to gain victory points. The use of investments to change control of countries, and the game-end value of your investments being the lion's share of your score, lift the game above Antike (good game though that is) and a lot of other Euro strategy games.

I love the constant flux within the game, as countries that were enemies become friends (and vice versa), and a countries that looked like  lions become pups (and vice-versa again).

While it's not cheap, the production values are excellent.

Warning: played with more than 4, players can find themselves without control of a country for long periods of the game. This doesn't stop them doing well if they invest wisely, but does make for two hours of extreme tedium for the affected player as they watch everyone else play the game and only get to make an investment decision every twenty minutes or so. I'd either stick with 4 players, or use the optional rule that gives players without a country an extra million to invest at investment time.

RATING: 10
I love money games... and I also liked playing Antike.  So when Imperial was showed up , I had to buy a copy.

Even though the designer is the same... the ONLY thing similar to Antike is the Rondel for action selection... the game is completely different.

As a person who likes a little chaos in his games... I should note that there is virtually NO control in this game.  The ownership of the major powers will change often and you really have to know what you are doing.  You can do well by not controlling a power... but it takes a lot of knowledge of the game to do so. The one saving grace is the 'Investor' card which allows you some chance to get back in the game (unlike other players who say otherwise, I would never play the game without it. The game becomes much too long!).

Can be an unforgiving game... but unlike games like Age of Steam where you have a very difficult time getting back into the game once you fall behind, you never seem to be completely "out-of-the-running."  One shrewd purchase can get you back into the game very quickly.

After playing again on BSW (with the optional start rules) my enjoyment of the game, and my rating, has gone up.

RATING: 10
I've enjoyed all the rondel games I've played, but this is clearly the best. Every country needs to balance investment with production, expansion with protection - and every player needs to decide when and what countries are worth investing in and how far to dive in. It feels like such a unique combination of military build up and dispassionate investment (dispassionate because getting too attached to a single country seems like a sure way to lose). Good investments piggyback on what other players have accomplished. My favorite games are those with relatively simple rules but support open, dynamic, complex and creative play. Imperial really fits my definition for a great game.

Players play major international investors who puchase the bonds of major countries in order to dictate what actions those countries perform. Whomever possess the highest bond in a country (whomever has paid that country the most money) controls that country. There are 6 european countries and players can control many or none, depending on how they've invested. It is the countries that take the turns, not the players. Countries build factories in order to produce ground or sea units to take over neutral countries (and collect taxes and points from them) or to disrupt the operations of other major powers (no money or points come from this action, though). Countries can also buy units (mercenaries) rather than producing them. Players can force countries to pay dividends to the bond holders (players) who have a stake in that country. And finally, the country can collect tax revenues. The more taxes a country collects, ultimately the more valuable that country is in victory points.

That's really all there is to it. The rest of the game is about countries using these actions to jockey for the tax dollars. The direction and focus that countries can take seem to be pretty broad. As players begin to invest in multiple countries, suddenly multiple interests are at stake, which leads to table talk and informal negotiations. You have to keep in mind that as a player you are not one country, but an investor in whatever countries you think will do well at the end of the game. You don't necessarily need to control a country to get a lot of points from it, but you do want to ensure your investments are protected and that your countries are giving you the return you expected. Control of countries can change hands multiple times. Final scores are determined by mulitplying the dividend amount on your country bonds by the power rating for that country (which only increases through taxation, remember). Then you add in left over player cash and declare a winner.

UPDATE: Upgraded from a 9 to 10. This game is a future classic. I can't think of anything that isn't perfect!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I enjoy watching the countries cycle through expansion and contraction, building successively bigger waves toward investment and taxation. Those who strive to hold onto to "their" country will find themselves frustrated by their inability to hold territories; those who wander aimlessly without ever controlling a country will miss out on the joy of creating those rhythmic financial waves. 

Throughout my plays I was reminded of Chicago Express. The two share stock-holding, investment, and portfolio diversification but elicit different emotional responses via their themes. Ultimately I prefer Chicago Express as it provides a similar experience in less than an hour and allows all investors to run operations.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After about 25 plays, have to go with this game as the best one of the recent year (going back to Essen 2006).  It makes excellent use of the Rondel device introduced by the same designer in Antike, but what makes this game succeed whereas Antike does not, is that the tension is there from the beginning as the pressure is on to invest in the best countries, to decide when to up and protect your investments, and when to let them go.  Despite the nice-looking toy cannons and boats, this is NOT a wargame.  It's primarily an investment/economic game, kind of a cross between 18xx and say, Diplomacy.  The game seems to start very slowly and take a long time to develop, but once nations start taxing in the 12-15 range, it can move quite rapidly.  Several variants have been suggested, but in my mind the game is just fine as it is.

RATING: 6
Imperial is an investing game with a conflict theme. Each player represents a wealthy banking family with heavy investments in one or more of the six pre-WWI Imperial powers. You treat the countries like companies, and your investments in them like a portfolio. Some military force (emphasis on the "some") boosts a country's economic position, but players who focus on conquest in control will lose badly. Watch your cash and your bond values. Look for ways to raise them. Make sure your stakes aren't bought out from under you at key moments. Invading territory is a relatively minor part of all this.

Update: I think I'm lukewarm Rondel games in general. Princes of Macchu Pichu is especially eye-opening in that regard, as the "multi-directional, exclusive rondel with clients" seems a whole lot more interesting. 

[b]Final Word[/b]: I have a begrudging respect for the design here, but the Investor Card is complete nonsense and the advanced game just is not that enjoyable for me. This is an ugly-slog-through-the-mud sort of game with few options.

RATING: 9
An absolutely sterling game that blends Euro elements with some nice direct conflict. The rondel mechanism is well implemented and not too restrictive; options seem well balanced on the "wheel" in just the right proportion and order. All the pieces seem to fit pretty well here, and there are some good decisions to be made. After a second play, in which I finished last (as opposed to winning the first game), my opinion of this game only improves. It's long but still quite relaxed and provokes some trash talking and quasi-"understandings" with your neighbors. I think the simplicity of the combat contributes in a good way to the lightness of the game--this is not a war game, folks--and neither combat nor economic expansion/development feels too powerful or important. The real strategy is to hitch your wagon to the right stars--ideally, the top three countries in terms of stock-value multiplier and to loot the countries for money, not become attached to them. Controlling blocs of countries can be extremely powerful, as you can, say, demilitarize an area; however, you can't count on keeping that bloc, and the larger the bloc the bigger the target on your back. True, the endgame is essentially the same every time, with the military campaigning ending in a rush to tax and invest, but even so the game doesn't feel samey; indeed, no two games have ever felt that similar, and none have lost my interest. In short, this is the best non-series game I've ever played, and it's clearly the acme of the rondel series.
