RATING: 8
Impressive game.  I bet that most people that approach this game make the same initial mistake that we did of playing it as a conquest game, especially after having played Antike.  About halfway through the first game, everything clicked and the strategies became more apparent.  I almost started thinking of it as Lost Cities (probably because of the colors), and I instantly regretted having too diverse of a bond portfolio.  There seems to be a fine line between building up your empires so that you can personally profit through interest on your bonds and letting others do the work for you, stepping in at an opportune time.  I like how the combat is simple and not always the most attractive option.  The game length is manageable, though I would like to get it down to well under 2 hours.  The game artwork is a little disappointing, with garish colors and unattractive design choices.  I look forward to more play, as I can see how the game will improve, due to better player choices and decreased play time.

RATING: 9.5
I like this game because the rules are simple and elegant, and yet they tangle up wonderfully to make a complex but fast-moving, fluid, strategic brain burner of perfect information, tension and frustration. 

Firstly, whatever you manage to do to advance the interests of one or other Great Power, your efforts may be turned against you as another financier takes over control of the government and your plans are turned to ash. 

Secondly, you want the government of any Great Power that you (temporarily) control to increase its tax base, because this increases the worth of its bonds - of which you hold the greatest number. There are two ways to increase tax: build factories, or send out armies and fleets to bring lands and seas under your government’s sway. But armies and fleets need to be paid, which reduces net tax. So you need to try to rid yourself of armies and fleets just before you tax the nation - which can only be done through war. Yes, you wage war, and suicidal war at that, just to get rich. Cynical? Moi?

But though wars make you rich, the game isn’t a war game, for all that there I sometimes sense a faint whiff of Risk. But it could hardly be further from Risk in its concept or mechanics. To start with, there are no dice, and luck plays no part. Countries are never owned outright; you may own a government for now, but someone else also has shares, and may buy enough bonds next turn to rob you of the ownership of the country entirely. The interplay of struggle on the board and struggle in the stock market makes for a rich and complex jumble of interactions between the players.

The theme works well, and the rules work well within the theme. The “tax” conceit doesn’t quite work thematically, however. The tax on foreign nations might be thought of as tribute, but what the tax on the ocean spaces might be is anyone’s guess - revenue from protected fisheries, perhaps, or from state-sponsored piracy?

This feels very much like a game that will grow on me the more I play it and the more I understand its complexities.

RATING: 1
It's a game of complete chaos, where the countries switch players constantly. One of the most amazing loopholes is that if you don't control a country you are able to manipulate the system and buy bonds when other people can't, so some of the players once they lost their country, played specifically not to get another one. It appears that the game designers were looking to use the game to influence people inductively to see chaos as normal, and order as unusual.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My comments are for the version of the game played without the Investor Card, which I strongly prefer.  In the version with the card, buying opportunities are too straight-jacketed for my preferences, and money slops around too freely due to the $2m subsidy given to the cardholder every time it's activated.  I don't play this version of the game.

Imperial provides the investment and capital management experience I was hoping to find in the 18xx, but did not.  Just as 18xx is a stock game that happens to be tethered to a railroad operations game, Imperial is a stock game that happens to be tethered to a simple war game.

In both instances, what happens on the board only serves to provide imbalances and fluctuations in the value of the stocks available for purchase.

And I like the conquest game in Imperial plays better than the railroading one in 18xx.  For one thing, Imperial allows for much more aggressive and zero-sum play.  A clever token drop just can't compare to a land invasion of Britain.  But there's plenty of clever alliances possible here, too.  

Imperial is streamlined, there's very little here but game, and every decision you make is meaningful. What bookkeeping there is is quick, and easy to do.  The Rondel provides for some extremely tough decisions:  build a Factory now and invest in the future, or Manauveure and go for the offensive now?  Hit Investor to dump out quick cash, or Produce to get some much-needed devensive units?

Timing is probably the most important aspect of playing this game well.  Anyone who's had a Flag taken from them by another investor just as that country was due a big president's taxation bonus can attest to that.

If I have any gripes about this game, it's that I sometimes wish there was a little more you could do with capital and the stock market... allow players to sell bonds back, for example, perhaps with an attendant push backward on the country's power chart?  Perhaps add something akin to 18xx's train rush with weapons technology, and allow presidents to shuffle money between national treasures?  I don't know whether this stuff could be added without unduly prolonging the game... perhaps this is the makings for another game, a sucessor to Imperial.

I expect to get a lot of play out of this.  I look forward to expansion maps, both from Mac Gerdts and ones that fans may come up with.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Europe in the age of imperialism before WWI.  Short playing time is a plus. A brilliant game. Each player is not a king or emperor in the game controlling a country to start fire and war but a loan-shark opportunist, who bets on winning side in a war and territorial expansion. The bond is inflated when people up and running for buying them. If a country lost the war and the treasury drained up, the bondholder would not have interest distribution from the country. You cannot, however, sell a bond you don't want. So make sure you only bet right on! 

The game design blends many facets of what was "Imperialism". Cash is king. Only greed is rewarded. Cash can control a nation government. A nasty game to some but the competitive player surely like it. It is not necessarily the military might you control wins. How to negotiate with your neighbours is also very important.

You can choose one of the 6 actions to take for your controlled nation by moving your token in the roundel space. You can build "Factory", which can produce army/ fleet for free if you choose the "Production" action. You can "Import" and deploy 3 armies for a fee (1 million, it sounds a lot but it ain't). You can "Maneuver" your units one region at a time. Sea transport can be performed with this action for moving offshore to another land area with fleet. Combat is entirely voluntary and if a war is declared, combat is resolved on a 1 to 1 army loss. Otherwise, units from different nations can coexist peacefully in an area. By spending 3 armies, you can destroy other nation's factory. You can also move your own unit within their country at will. 

You can "Invest". It happens when any one player's nation token passes through the "Investor" space on the roundel. It pays out interest to each player who owns bonds of the token. It is the moment in time everybody happy. The player holding the "investor card" receives 2 million from bank (not controlled by any player) and then use the persal money to purchase any nation bond currently available. Bond face value only goes up when the lower face value bond has been purchased by other players. But remember, you can always purchase the higher bond value if you have the sufficient money. Controlling the highest total sums of bond of a nation means that the player gets hold of the nation card denoting complete control - the player controls the nation token movement on the roundel. If a player doesn't have a nation card in hand, he can also invest once in this action. 

Finally, you can "Tax" you nation subjects...poor commoners. National taxxes are comprised of 2 mllion per factory and 1 million per tax chip. Tax chip is placed in a neutral region (and only neutral region, no tax chips can be placed in foreign nation's home provinces/ areass) the when you have the nation troop in the same. Expanding your tax base increases your power on the Taxation Chart revenue level. Extra bonus is paid when the revenue leve is increased. Of course, your armies get feed at this time as well by deduction from the revenue you get. Play continues clockwise until one player's power reaches revenue level 25 on the Taxation chart. 

Imperial is the most satisfying experience I had with the Euro games so far. It is a war of money. It gives you the insight into how Imperialism works and why war is waged. I want to buy the game and it is a good sign. I should also revisit one of the Paradox Entertainment PC game for the era to recap all the good moment. Unlike the PC game, Imperial can be finished in one day for sure. A great system and a great game indeed. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I was expecting this since I played it for the first time, and, after more plays, I can safely give it a 10. Just brilliant. Before playing this game, I simply could not imagine that a game dealing with economy could be fun. Well, I guess I was incredibly wrong. This is a masterpiece from a guy who seems to never put a foot wrong - everything he designs goes straight to the top 1000. 

I must mention this is one of my most played games, behind Tigris&Euphrates only. I tried all player counts except two, playing with or without the investor card and both draft vs. bidding for the first distribution of shares and I must say I absolutely love all variations. What I find best is playing with 4-5 people and starting the game with bidding for the shares. As opposed to most people in the Investor card debate, I don't particularly prefer one over the other. They are different, but both are interesting.

I wrote a review about it that got a secondary prize at the Voice of Experience contest: [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1592916/voice-experience-imperial-gateway-heavier-drugs-su]Imperial as a gateway to heavier drugs, such as Poseidon[/url]

RATING: 8
In gambling the only winner is the bookie

In war the only winner is the profiteer

In peace the only winner is the person who understands the tax system

If you can combine all three then you may have a good chance in Imperial. 

Solid design, often inspired.  Strong theme, clear structure and excellent use of the increasingly popular rondel combines to produce an absorbing game especially as working out tax returns only takes minutes.

Empires will rise and fall within the three hours for this game therefore it is recommended for groups of players who enjoy the long term and who have a knack for finance.

RATING: 8
I think that had I not played Indonesia first, I'd have rated this even higher, but Indonesia is more tightly integrated. With that said, Imperial is a delightful experience, requiring some thought but not overwhelmingly so, with terrific components (the money and shares both appeal to my tactile sense), and surprisingly easy to understand and play. One thing to avoid, however, is having war gamers around the table; they will cause the game to drag endlessly in pointless aggressive military moves that contribute nothing to gameplay. Also, the game is emotionally difficult to handle that first time someone takes control of "your country", so folks must be warned of this strongly in advance or their experience will be ruined.

RATING: 10
First plays might feel a bit chaotic since you are so unused to the mechanism where you do play one country through out the game since you can loose control when other players are investing in it. But since that settled in your mind the struggling for maiking those countries you favour in the game becomming the most successful together with the amount of interaction is awesome.

It was the first game with the rondell mechanism for chosing actions I encoutered and I think it is outstanding and it speeds up a game that otherwise could have been to long it gaming time. Since you constantly have to keep up with what other players are doing there is not much feeling of down time.
I think the only thing that could lower my rating is when i try Imperial 2030.

RATING: 10
One of the best games ever.  It improves upon Diplomacy by adding a moderately deeper military system with economics.  Yet it still stays simple.  And honestly, the idea of not controlling a country, but instead being an investor who can control whatever country in which they invest the most money is genius.  

This mechanic alone always keeps me on edge since it is so foreign to how I normally think, especially in games.  I constantly have to remind myself that I can ditch countries that are not doing well, or not control any countries at all, and still win.  And any game that challenges the way I think is a slam dunk in my opinion.

RATING: 8
Play the role of an unethical investor : get rich as much as you can ; (ab)use armies to your own profit and buy shares in a clever manner.
I like the cynical theme  ; just reality ! 

Game is best without investor card ;  so you have more impact on the game
Only slightly luck dependend ; you cannot win on your own. You  have to take profit  of the (unwilling cooperation !) greed of others to win. 
Timing and choice of the share  you will  buy is crucial for winning.
+ unpredictable game in the beginnning ; every game is totally different
you have a clear start stage, middle and endgame ; just like in chess.
- game turns are a bit repetitive ; investor and taxation space are the most important actions ; other spaces on the rondel only serve as cosmetic help for your own country or to make other countries less attractive for potential investors.
- very fragile game ; if you make a mistake at game start ; it's sometimes impossible to compete anymore ; sometimes kingmaking can be a factor if someone is excluded from winning at the start of the game

RATING: 8
Stock game with an element of war in terms of area control. Some countries are better than others due to board position. Works really well with 4 players, 6 is way too much.

When you own a country, you are in charge and responsible for other share holders. We haven't yet tried to play without the Investor card, but will definitely try with it the next time to reduce the owner swings. I really like this game and I think our group hasn't really grasped the depth of it yet.

RATING: 6
My biggest disappointment so far. I really wanted to like this game, but ultimately "Imperial" delivers a "broken" feeling. If a player is left with no flag and thus cannot form a government, he becomes very powerful. So, although I have to acknowledge that a player with no government is not eliminated (as someone would expect), that player can pay no attention whatsoever of what is happening on the board and just buy the right bonds. He only have to be careful not to form a government ever again. Solution? The players must never leave another player without a flag, but then a neat aspect of the game (governments changing hands) is mitigated. So, I conclude that 3 is the right number of players because then everyone starts with 2 governments. Additionaly you must always, ALWAYS, play without the investor card.
Another thing I dislike is how the "Investor" and "Taxation" options are abused at the end of the game. 
In conclusion, when I have to play a game only in a certain way in order to be playable and enjoyable and I can manipulate the mechanics (and not the theme) of a game in order to win, I consider this game defective.
Furthermore, this is not a wargame (that's not necessarily bad). It is a mediocre economic game.

RATING: 9
A game where you buy stock in pre-WWI nations. With ownership you buy guns, expand, and tax to increase the nations wealth. The game is NOT area control, but instead who ever has the most stock values in nations that are doing well at the end wins. It allows you to buy into the wining nation.

RATING: 7
Flavor-wise, it's strange that each of these Great Powers tax their people and pay (usury level) interest so irregularly, and on the whim of a single investor. It's as if the only people with a will of their own are the players. Also, the art looks awkward. Other than that, it's business as usual.

The stock system provides for some natural alliances that last throughout the game, which makes it more complex and interesting than a simple hit-and-run backstabathon. Sometimes it has a tendency toward locking some people out for extended periods and making it difficult to catch up.

Overall, a solid, if long, game.

RATING: 10
5 / 5 - Great 

There's quite a staggering amount going on here in terms of aspects of strategy to analyze. so much so that it prevents over-analysis and AP by pushing players out of the other side. The combination of economic management on diplomacy style unit maneuvering is unusual, interesting and cleverly integrated, although it should be noted that the economic side of the game is primary. The turn order that results from the bid-for-countries mechanic can sometimes lead to periods of downtime for one or two players. RATING: 9
I played this game only once and I was very satisfied.

In a nutshell, it's Diplomacy but you don't control the states: you control the fat cats who fund the states. These greedy capitalists treat entire nations like companies whose shares can be bought in the stock exchange.

Who pays the piper calls the tune and who owns the bonds sets the policy. These avid oligarchs get filthy rich by investing in the right nation in the right moment and/or having the states they control prevail over the others.

RATING: 7
I find it amazing that this game is ranked so highly.  It is an interesting theme.  Players are arms merchants for the Great Powers before WW1 and use the governments as proxies for their purposes.  It seems to be an abstraction of Diplomacy with an extra feature - the ability to flip control of a government.  The problem I have with the game is that it lacks the grace of Diplomacy.  The sweeping attacks, the support (and betrayal), and the simultaneous movement.  I think it is a fine game - just not what I wanted or hoped for.  I've played three times now and each game has been close (no arguments about the balance - it is balanced) and there are good Machiavellian moments.  Still, I wish the movement rules were more like Diplomacy.  Fleets in this game are certainly not as effective.  Oh well. Rant over.

RATING: 7
Game in a Nutshell: Players control nations by the purse strings then send them to war in order to profit.

Played as: 6 player

Pros: Very nice blend of war and euro games. I really get a sense that I'm running a country by either building it up or rather paying out to my cronies as it falls into the pits. The rules are pretty smooth, with very few niggly details. Ripe for diplomacy if you'd like some viciousness. Overall, just a very thematic game.

Cons: I don't love how you can sit around not controlling a nation for often huge chunks of time. It's like being eliminated from the game only you can't get up and start another game because the design wants you to sit and hope for the chance to jump back in. I ask myself if player elimination wouldn't have been more merciful. The rondel, while a neat mechanic, just makes little thematic sense in a thematically heavy game. 

Other: So I botched a couple rules in my only play that lengthened the game and stripped out a couple key elements, but still found it very interesting and immediately would have played again (and that's with not yet realizing the errors). That has to count for something. I'd think the game would be better with a mid-range of players where competition is high but there's enough countries that you're less likely to be eliminated early.

Best Use: There's not a lot of multi-player heavier weight Wa-euros out there. This gets high honors in that category.

RATING: 8
Excellent & elegant stock game where players own stock in countries and take the actions for the countries they own the majority of stock in.  The actions are selected from the (Antike) rondelle.  As countries are built up their stock produces larger dividends and their end-game multiplier improves.  I think this may have a lot of depth and replayability.  UPDATE: knocked rating down 1/2 a point... there may be some control issues with the timing of the investor card, especially with the end game.  Something to be aware of so you can try to plan for it.  The "expert" setup where players take turns buying bonds to begin with is the way to go.

RATING: 7
My best description would be economic Diplomacy, in that players can buy controlling shares in countries out from under each other.

It took some time to grok that I was NOT the country I was playing but an investor, and I did feel like there was a bit of a runaway leader problem in the game. I can see that if all players do not play optimally (and the player who I was paired with due to having shares in the same countries was NOT playing optimally) than it can create some game balance issues. Still, the entire game was an absolute blast - very tense, and engrossing.

In the end, however, I still feel like I have a similar experience with Chicago Express, in about 1/4th of the time, and there is less chaos to manage in CE.

RATING: 6
The game is excruciatingly long with players who think it's a wargame with money. It becomes fairly quick but tedious with players who think it's just about the cash. Then it becomes long again with players who realize the primacy of cash but try to eke out little advantages here and there through the wargame aspect. At this point some veterans claim the game transforms into a "negotiation" game. Fair enough.

Despite my lack of excitement, I remain intrigued enough by this title to want to play it every once in a while.

I think I prefer this board to [boardgame=54138][/boardgame]'s, as it gives each country a more distinct feel and forces more confrontational play.

RATING: 10
Previously I often played conflict games, and I still appreciate their strategic and diplomatic turns, but lately I've moved on to economic games, where plain rate of return rather than (un-)faithfulness against allies dictate the dynamics. Imperial is a game that appeals both to generals and businessmen. At the surface, it's a war between military organizations and production machineries where whoever can bring armies and fleets at the right time to the right place wins. But beneath this surface the true game hides: capitalists that unscrupulously support and abandon the nations depending on the expected return of the war.

The players invest money in nations that those in turn spend on factories to produce armies and fleets with the purpose of conquering territories. Success on the battle field means more tax to the nation and higher value to the investor. The actions are chosen through a simple but elegant rondel mechanism where every action is important to build the war economy but also delays the for the victory important tax action.

This mechanism would have been enough for a "normal" conflict game but the greatness of Imperial is the fact that the nations constantly change owners. Is a nation doing well? Invest in it for a takeover to reap the rewards yourself. Is a nation doing badly? Use it as battering ram to further other nations and abandon it. All is fair in war and business.

Imperial reminds a bit about the 18XX games with its shareholder economy and total lack of randomness but has a better flow in the game thanks to simpler rules and short but meaningful turns. The players may not be able to dump nations on each other but this is compensated by the shifting alliances on the board as the ownerships are changing. The result is a swingy game but not unpredictable if you understand the connection between the players' motives and the game's mechanisms. Imperial is simply a game where the players drive the game instead of the other way around, just like it should be in a good game. To fully understand this connection is another issue...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
In 19th century Europe, on a map similar to Diplomacy, there are a number of bonds associated with the 6 countries on the map: Austria-Hungary, Italy, France, England, Germany and Russia. Players controlling the most bonds of a country take actions for the country by moving along a circular rondel. The actions are: raise armies/navies, move units, tax the country and invest in the country. In the basic game, there is an investor card that rotates clockwise and allows the holder to buy bonds in any country. In the advanced game, once a country has finished the turn, anyone can buy bonds in that country. The bonds acts as victory point multipliers, ranging from 1 to 5, depending how far the country has advanced in power points. Designed by Mac Gerdts, the system is similar to his previous game, Antike. This is a wargame, so it will not be for everyone. Sometimes nations get attacked without much provocation. Also the nations start in a particular order (Austria, then Italy moves, etc.) This can mean some players will be short a turn. I think being a wargame, this game relies on players to balance the game. 

Now, too be good at this game, you have to treat the countries like a stock market and invest in the one that looks like it will succeed. I'm not very good at this, I tend to get drawn into the tactical aspects, and I'm not that good at it either! It has some interesting concepts, but it is a bit long, and it's not my type of game.

RATING: 8
心得:
*重點1 在一個國家收稅後會強烈成長前想辦法拿到控制權，收稅成長值不高的不需要特別搶
*重點2 小股票其實CP值很高。未必須要搶國家控制權，主要是看買之後賺的比例，最後期蠻好算的
*不能隨時買股票其實蠻卡的，真的要規劃的好點
規則:
*長柱放右上行動輪中央，短柱放下方分數軌0，跟右上稅務軌2-5，每個國家起始在其方格城市放一間工廠，放對應顏色，債券根據國家分類，數字小的在上面，放對應位置
*4-6人時每人拿13圓，國家牌洗勻每人發一張，3人時起始24圓，奧地利-英國/義大利-俄國/德國-法國，分成三組隨機發，2人時每人35，法國/德國/奧地利一組，另外三國一組發，每人跟去國家牌後面拿對應債券，並支付對應金錢給該國，最後剩2圓，每個擁有該國債券總值最高的玩家拿該國國旗卡
*起始變體2/3/4/5/6人拿40/28/22/18/15圓，隨機起始，依序從奧地利>義大利>法國>英國>德國>俄國買債券，每次買時由起始買該國的一張，然後再輪一圈，然後起始左傳換下個國家，沒有拿到國家牌的拿瑞士牌
*遊戲輪流由國家行動(奧地利>義大利>法國>英國>德國>俄國)，每次行動最多長柱移動6格(起始隨意格)，超過3格的每格要付兩圓，遊戲在一國分數超過25時結束，玩家加總現金跟債券利率乘上該國力量參數的錢，平手比擁有最高分國家債券總額，依序比下去
*如果有國家一開始沒人有該國國旗卡，則行動跳過，起始投資卡給奧地利控制國家玩家的左手玩家，否則給義大利左手邊玩家
*玩家錢跟國家錢分開，但是玩家錢可用來幫國家支付，國家錢公開，玩家錢隱藏
>工廠 付五圓蓋一棟在沒有敵兵的對應顏色城市地點上
>生產 每個工廠沒敵兵的免費產1兵(棕)或1船(藍)
>進口 花一元買一兵/船放到沒有敵兵的自己城市，最多三個，可重複放，船只能放港口
>行軍 所有船先可移動一格(港口到相鄰海域算一格)，之後移動步兵一格(瑞士不能進去)，過海要有船，每艘船可傳一隻>放一個國家旗子在最後佔領沒有其他國單位的頂土或海洋上
*步兵在自己的家園五格內可自由移動通過沒有敵兵的領土不算格數
*步兵跟船，移動到有其他國家單位的地方時任何一方可選擇開戰，一換一換掉，只有在行軍時可選擇開戰即使不移動，步兵如果到其他國家領土但是選擇倒下則不算敵兵，一個國家剩最後一個工廠沒有敵兵時，就不能再被占領，三個步兵可換掉一個工廠(要沒有守軍，最後一個一樣不能拆)
*船在港口時可以跟兵開戰
>投資  每個有國家債券的玩家從該國家拿對應金錢(如果不夠付，有該國國旗的人不少拿，在不夠有該玩家支付)>有投資卡玩家獲得兩圓並可以買一張債券，可買新的或升級舊的，之後投資可左傳(升級付差值)>有瑞士銀行卡但沒有投資卡的也可買一次(多人時照順位，從有投資卡的開始)，之後決定誰有哪張國家卡，加總債券卡利息，多的拿，平手順位靠近投資卡玩家的拿，沒國家的一樣拿瑞士銀行卡
*如果行動轉盤經過投資不停，則先結算最後停的行動，之後再結算投資付債券以外的行動
*有瑞士銀行卡的玩家可強迫其他玩家停留在投資行動，前提時該玩家夠錢付利息
>收稅 每個沒敵兵工廠兩圓，領土旗子一圓，記錄在稅務軌，超過15放15，如果新的值比之前高，超過多少該國家卡擁有者拿多少錢(變少不扣錢)>根據稅務軌右邊數字增加該國的分數軌>之後稅務值扣除場上兵數，該國家拿對應金錢，負的就不拿
*如果加分數時超過25分，即使該回合有經過投資者也不結算
***無投資卡變體   不使用投資卡，每個國家回合結束後照順序每人可投資該國一次，每有國家卡的人可在任何時候投資任何國家
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The rondel of actions forces each of the 6 countries through cycles of troop expansions, deployment and payouts. The gameplay is to determine which countries are going to be safest from attack (because the players invested in that country control neighbouring countries) and then invest in it as much as you can because it can cycle to the payout phase quicker rather than having to spend turns defending. The other aim is to build semi-alliances with other players - having multiple investors in a country you control diminishes the chances of it being attacked. There are good decisions to be had when choosing the next rondel action for a country. It's dullsville when you don't have a country. As a result, the game might shine with experienced players, but it's a harsh beast for those players who fail to develop a controlling interest or fail to build helpful alliances. The end of game scoring can also be harsh when being just 1 turn slow can make a big difference in the multiplier you receive. It's a game I respect, but not one I overly enjoy due to its harshness and over-reliance on reading alliances well.

RATING: 9
Rating is based on using the no investor card variant and the experienced players setup.

Excellent. The movement and combat resemble Diplomacy, but it's about making smart investments rather than area control. Many people become too attached to countries they control and stumble into making errors like waging wars of attrition instead of making money. To win, players must correctly assess which countries will be profitable and invest in them. Controlling or at least predicting when the game will end is also important. No luck and very little downtime.  

A lot of playtesting and development went into this game, and it shows. The rulebook is clear and the rules are simple but the gameplay is deep.

RATING: 6
The gaming time was longer than I expected. The system needs a while to get used to: no more you have your own country which you defend. Now anyone can have any country to his/her control with sufficient money investment. Moves can be rather surprising with that element...

Well, the game went on in an ok pace but with investor-phase, we had a problem: thinking which share to buy... then it gets tricky. You first have to calculate your money and then the remaining shares and then check the situation between countries and revise the rules and check the situation again... For a first-time player, the movement of money from countries to players can be quite a mess. It gets better during the game, but at first, it's a shock.

It was surprising that there are basically no fight for the countries' color-coded areas, only sea-areas and neutral landscapes. It was a nice addition, since now there was no use turtling too much.

Downtime was sometimes too much. I contributed to downtime also as much as anyone else but still.. I'd prefer a shorter version of this all, since I really liked the basic system in this. Rondel fits also to this game like a nose to a gas mask!

RATING: 8
Have just played twice and think it's a very fun game.  But, the MINIMUM time for 3 - 4 players is about 5 hours.  Advice to novices: it's an ECONOMIC game, not a war game, so don't just make war with others.  Excellent blend of Risk and Diplomacy where the catch is that you don't run a country, you invest in a country, and once you become majority stock holder in a country you get to run it.  Which means countries change hands throughout the game.  Brilliant design and fun game.

One 3-player and one 6-player with Investor card. Plays quickly even with 6 players. Keep control of at least one country and try to pay out early when you have dominant control. Avoid fighting on any more fronts than absolutely necessary. Some areas just 'belong' to a country, moving in will likely produce a protacted counter-productive war. A player that doesn't understand this will likely drag down both himself and his unlucky neighbor. Early less-than-optimal play will assure a less than stellar finish. Understanding comes quickly but lost opportunities early can't be made up.

RATING: 10
According to Lenin the highest form of capitalism is Imperialism - the stage wherein the pursuit of capital transcends national borders and creates geopolitical conflict through the exploitation of militant Nations to conquer and tax other nations. 
With the rise of Imperialism, world wars become marketing vehicles for Nations to show off their power and convince international investors to buy war bonds.

Through relatively simple mechanics, Imperial is able to deliver to us an amazingly nuanced (and fun!) first-hand demonstration of Lenin's philosophy.
Honestly, regardless of if the real world implications curdle your blood or not, Imperial ought to be taught in every school.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
This is a stock market game with a wargame pasted on, and neither the stock market game nor the wargame are any good.  It amounts to hours of pointless fiddling around with bits that have essentially no overall impact on the value of the stocks, and a sudden final result that, despite being entirely deterministic, is essentially a purely random result of chaos. I played it twice, bored stiff both times. The first, I finished dead last after spending most of the game unable to do anything: "No, I can't do anything; I don't have any money and don't control any of the countries so I have no way to get any." The second, I [i]won[/i] by a significant margin, after spending most of the game unable to do anything. (There were several times I could see that I wouldn't need to [i]look at the board[/i] for the next twenty minutes, and in fact I NEVER touched any of the countries' pieces during the whole game -- and still won.) Another player was so bored she left in the middle of it (and she NEVER leaves a game that way), but SHE came in 3rd place out of 6.  Fiddling the bits around on the board may seem amusing until you realize it's all a pointless waste of time and has no effect on the outcome, due to the unrealistic and artificial scoring mechanisms. I could make this into a decent wargame so easily using the same bits that it's tragic to waste all that good material on such a useless boring game. The only reason I didn't rate it "2" is that it's conceivable someone I really like could twist my arm enough to make me play it again. It's just not likely. I think all the "10" ratings are from people who haven't yet realized how pointless it is to bother pushing the pieces around the board -- they THINK they're playing a wargame or something, because they haven't given the game itself any thought. The player comments from those rating it 5 or below show far more insight into the game. --- UPDATE: No, I decided it's inconceivable for anyone to talk me into playing this game.  If I liked them enough for them to talk me into playing it, I would talk them OUT of playing it.

RATING: 9
I am really bad at Imperial.  It took a me awhile to even understand the rules (luckily there was a long break before this game started so one of the other players worked with me to help me learn it).  It's hard to adjust to thinking about investing rather than thinking area control.  I love the rondel, but like all the mechanics in this game, it takes a while to figure out what you should be doing.  I'd definately play Imperial again because I'd like to wrap my brain around the really cool mechanics.  
****Update****
My rating for Imperial has improved as I play it more because it is a game that seriously punishes the new player.  I would recommend playing it with people who are at about the same skill level that you are.  It's hard to play Imperial when everyone is a new player, but it's no fun to play Imperial when everyone except you has played it many times.  

Also, my rating for Imperial only holds with more than three players.  With three I'd say Imperial is a eight at best.  But with five or six players, Imperial is a shinny penny!  Even if it takes a lot longer to play, each country becomes fully engaged in the game.  With three players, usually all of the players have one country that they are using as a shield or a prop for the countries that they are actually using to create their hopefully winning strategies. That leaves three nations to be weak and underplayed.  That's half that game that is unengaged!  I don't want to play Imperial like that.  I want each decision to be one that I care about.  With three players, half of the decisions I make don't matter to me or to anyone else.  Is this a flaw of the game? No.  I'd just say that Imperial isn't meant to be played with three because it is certainly one hell of a game with five or six!

***Musings***

I hated Imperial after the first play. It wasn't until the second play that I realized that there was something interesting with Imperial. Imperial is exciting and a trip, but it's disorienting and unpleasant on the first play. Each play perplexes me more and more. It's a brilliant game.  (If you've read this far and haven't played Imperial, what are you waiting for?)

RATING: N/A
Monopoly meets Risk where it isn't about how much territory you conquer, but rather how much money you've made at the end.  Gone over well with every group I have played with so far.

After numerous plays, I have begun to see the only flaw in this great game is the purchase of bonds that control the governments.  The values run from 1 to 8, but a player can buy up all the lower and cheaper bonds thus effectively preventing the other players from mounting an attempt to take control of a country.

Usually, this event happens when one player sees which country is doing well and then does the same thing to two or three other countries.  By that time, it is obvious they are going to win the game.  So after three hours of playing, I am wondering why the heck did I just waste this time over the game.

This problem wouldn't be so bad if players were all inexperienced in this game; however, once everyone gains enough experience.  It typically becomes a race to snatch up the leading country while two or three other players are just sitting around with little money or control of countries wondering what to do for the next hour and a half until the games ends.

RATING: 6
Very unique Euro game with a lot of direct player interaction, in which you only temporarily control different nations (and sometimes none of them at all). While I acknowledge the clever design, I am not a huge fan of this game. 

Being investors is a nice concept, but the restrictions to only being able to invest, when you have the investor card and that you are limited to only one security, feels very gamey and is a big downturn to me. I also dislike the Rondell mechanisms, since it gives a false impression of an annual cycle. Since you just can skip paying interests or collecting taxes, this feels wrong to me. Especially that skipping interest payments is possible, makes being only a minor creditor much more unattractive.

This thematic inconsistencies aside, this game has a lot of interesting decisions. Should you invest more in the country you control or should take over another country? Go for taxes now or expand you influence zones first? But there is also a lot of downtime in the game, especially if you only control one or even no country. 

In total a very good game, which is just not up my alley.

RATING: 7.72
Essentially the same game as Impeal 2030. Players 'invest' in countries which fight wars in early twentieth century Europe, while the players try to extract money and ensure they have the most shares in the succesful nations.
My first play of this was with two players who were taking it more seriously than a game with several inexperienced players and a newbie should be. It reminded me why Euro-games avoid player interaction. If you are upset by another players error or mis-judgement harming your position sufficiently that it spoils the game you are not going to enjoy this. Players can, and probably should directly attack each other. When you mess up it will hurt other players and offer opportunities for others to exploit. I like that, but I recognise that it takes a certain psychological readiness to handle it and the game can break down when some players don't have that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Probably the best economic game I've played.  The fighting on the board is great in that you don't feel like you are getting attack, but rather just a country that you are more heavily invested in, which removes the feelings of being personally attacked that can arise in multiplayer wargames.  It's a good implementation of a rondel as well, in that it really just limits you from doing some of the key actions like invest and taxation twice in a row, but being able to spend money to push further along the rondel gives you enough choice that the rondel is not limiting.  Turns really go very quickly - the only real downtime is when someone needs to decide what country bond to invest in, since it's hard to plan ahead exactly how much money you're going to have and the relative value of each country when your turn comes up to buy.  If you like stock games like Airlines Europe and Chicago Express, and also like multiplayer wargames like Game of Thrones, this is the perfect mixture of the 2. I'd also add that it's one of those games that doesn't take any longer with more players, since it's really the 6 countries taking turns and the controlling player just deciding what the country does, rather than each player taking turns.

RATING: 8
I't a good idea to incorporate the Swiss bank and rondel costs from Imperial 2030 into this.

----------
Not as complicated as I'd feared and an absorbing two hours. You just have to pay attention that the money is flowing in all the right directions. An interesting role playing the investor. Although many investments tend to lose you money in the final scoring they can be justified by how you used the country's forces and position on the rondel.

You don't want to get too far out ahead in owning a country as it's good to have fellow investors with a vested interest in keeping your country steaming ahead. It can also be a good idea to hold back your cash with some smaller investments and then jump ahead at an opportune moment.

To play with or without the investor card, that is the question. I see advantages and disadvantages, but ultimately the increase in speed of play wins out with me in favour of the investor card. Only play with the advanced setup and if players are not experienced then I wouldn't advise playing with more than four players.

Gamers seem to have a love/hate relationship with this one. Some players find the swinging ownership in a country and how that can ruin the best laid plans hard to come to terms with. Also, the real possibility that they won't control a country for a length of time can be boring. With players who enjoy the game it can be a wonderful experience, for others it should be avoided. I've experienced a dud game with players who haven't grasped the 18XX nature of the game. I really like it though. There's plenty of interesting choices. Lots happening on the board. The pace of the game is nice and quick.

RATING: 9
Mac Gerdts took the historically important and outsized role that bond markets have often played in warfare and turned it into two brilliant board games (Imperial and Imperial 2030). These games teach the valuable lesson that since the advent of professional (and thus very expensive) armies the world's bankers and financiers have been as important to waging successful war than the generals and guns. My favorite Gerdts game (together with Imperial 2030). Great games!

Now this was a surprise.  I don't like Antike much - the Rondel is the one interesting feature - so hearing about the next game to use this idea didn't fill me with joy.  But the simple notion of having all the countries in the game all the time but their control switching hands at regular intervals is genius. RATING: 6
The game is most like Antike, though it doesn't remind me of that as I've never played. And, then, it's also like the 18xx games, which I've also never played, in that the game isn't so much about conflict or controlling territory as it is in controlling bonds and having influence behind the scenes in several countries. It's sort of like World War 0.5, modeling the financial wrangling behind-the-scenes that likely led to armed conflict in the end. 

You could easily retheme the game to be about the Cola Wars, the European edition, with shareholders backing several Pop manufacturers as they work to increase market share across the continent. From the outside, it looks like the companies are fighting bitter battles to win customers in various countries but behind the scenes the shareholders, with interests spread across several "competing" brands, are colluding to increase the value of their shares: much like, I suspect (in my more conspiracy-minded moods), happens in the boardrooms of Pepsi, Coca Cola, and are there any other companies? Sometimes, I wonder if there's just one giant soda company with several brands: like a hydra with each head lashing toothlessly at the others so that noone notices they're all attached to the same body. 

Anyway, that's how the game felt to me. I think the Cola War theme would have actually worked better. It's a game for financiers, not a game for brigadiers. In either case, it's not a game for me. It's not bad, the game is good for what it is and not so long as to become painful, but it just didn't excite me very much. I could be convinced to play again, but I wouldn't seek it out.

RATING: 5
Played at BGG.con '06. I'm giving it a 4.5 rating because I was really dissappointed - I thought I'd like it a lot more than I did. Antike was my favorite game of the con in '05, and this sounded like it was similar, but with a stock-control type of mechanic. That much is true, but there are some aspects of the game that I have major problems with. In particular, the Invester tile, which moves from player to player clockwise (turns are taken in country order, not player order, with the majority stockholder taking the country's turn). I am not happy with the idea that I can't buy stock to take over the country I want to use (based on board opsition) because my left hand opponent will simply take it back (because he gets the Investor tile next).

Buying stock is such a major factor, why couldn't the investor tile move at some time or in some way related to the game? Maybe when you cross the Investor line on the Rondell (which is when the Investor tile triggers), the player moving there should take the tile or something? I dunno, just something that's not so metagame related would be nice.

I really wanted to like this game. I may have to try again with the variant rules I've heard mentioned.

Edit: Finally played again... WITHOUT the Investor card, and liked it a lot better. Still not my favorite type of game, but it wasn't as horrible as the last time. Upgraded rating to 5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Hard to rate. 

I needed a year to find the players who would buy the premise of the game (they didn't, I just didn't tell what we're going to play). And all of them kinda dozed off through the rules explanation (which are fairly simple). Than the first portion of the game happened which was marked by "mine, mine" attitude of owning a country and fighting and tension in the air. They I started milking my country and took me a couple of turns to convince the owner of another one which I had shares in, to do the same. This was a lightbulb moment and a game changed into something else, people were buying stocks, not fighting anymore as they were involved in both sides of the conflict. Suddenly the game was much more peaceful, friendly even. And the second threshold was reached when I tried to figure out how to go from taxation to investor as fast as it went. From then on it was a tense race of buying last stocks, speculation and race to finish or (more commonly) stalling. 

The description shows that the game slowly reveals its charms and I'm left with an impression there are many more thresholds to reach, that there is a still a lot of mystery to discover. The game absolutely earns respect and did create interesting interaction effects even on its first game. And there are 4 more variants existing - the non-investor card version (probably better than with investor), longer set up version (probably better with more experience), swiss bank variant (fun), and negotiation variant (for when you think you know the game). 

My only worry is that I might not play it enough to see all of it. The premise doesn't sound charming and the visuals are unpleasant at the least, so it's hard to bring it to the table. And the game is puzzling to people who are used to quickly consume a game and get it in one or two tries. (The copy was borrowed from owner who played it once, and probably never again will). I think it's a marvel of a game that was long time in creation and it shows by the organic feel of it, its depth and many possible variants to play. Here's to hope I can play it more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Imperial is a finely crafted, dynamic, luckless, in-your-face economic game. Luckless does not mean without chaos, though, and people tend to either like or dislike Imperial based on how reactive it is. From a strategic perspective, Imperial doesn't really allow a player to pick a strategy and stick with it. In the early game, you must be a generalist with respect to strategy, positioning yourself soundly in the financial interests of a few nations, and working to develop a nation that you control. I haven't really determined whether I like or dislike this about Imperial: the fact that winning often comes down to the last few rounds of play. To be sure, to be in a *position* to win, you must make good decisions in the early and middle game, but I don't think you need to make *optimal* decisions. Just good, solid ones. Then it all comes down to how deftly you play the end game. So far, I think I like how this plays out. Every player seems to have a viable shot at winning, so long as s/he plays fairly well early on.

Overall, I really like this game. Imperial is an economic game at heart, and even though it features soldiers and ships, it isn't a war game. There are plenty of opportunities for military conflict though, and used judiciously, doing so can really help advance your plans. But becoming too militaristic will hurt you and drain your resources. You must stay focused on developing your financial holdings. Each player in Imperial is an investor, and you buy bonds in different countries. He who holds the most bonds in a country controls that country. But ownership can and does change throughout the game via what amount to a series of hostile takeovers.

There are a variety of things going on in Imperial. While you try to improve whichever country you control, you are also trying to invest other well-managed countries, make money for yourself, and block other players from becoming too powerful. And you must strive to achieve all this in a constantly shifting battlefield as other players' choices significantly change the game. Imperial is a medium weight game in my opinion, which sometimes dips into heavy territory near the end when you are calculating which decisions will yield the best outcomes. Things about the game I really like:

1. It's very dynamic - the situation is always developing and you must be flexible to take advantage of new opportunities.
2. There doesn't seem to be a kingmaker problem - everyone is in the running to win.
3. Play is in-your-face, with national takeovers and using military units to occupy (shut down) opponent factories and what not. I like the combat aspects of the game. Frankly, in a game about the ascendance and decline of nations, Imperial's combat rules capture area control exactly right - to my way of thinking.
4. The rules, the system, the components...everything is very elegant. There aren't any rules which feel like clutter. Because of this, Imperial is easy to teach and easy to remember between games. No re-learning rules required.
5. Pretty board and pieces! Very satisfying from a tactile and/or visual perspective.
6. Although I like luck, no luck in this game is a bonus. You really succeed or perish based on your decisions. Player choice introduces all needed chaos.

The major criticism against Imperial is that it is too reactive. I can't tell yet whether it even allows discretely different strategies. This isn't a dream game for "builder" type players who like to plan something out carefully and then create it without allowing anyone else to mess with it. I'm thinking of the way you build your own factory in Factory Fun or your own palace in Alhambra. Imperial also fails to capture the balance between strategy and tactics that something like Go achieves. Imperial doesn't reward strategic geniuses. It rewards tactical geniuses who have solid strategic skills. And in the end, it's details like this which will often determine who likes the game and who doesn't. For my money though, it is an outstanding game that blends interesting mechanisms so that the whole surpasses the mere sum of parts - unlike a game such as Yspahan which throws together a bunch of gamey mechanisms and ends up with a whole equal to *precisely* the sum of its parts.

Update: I remain concerned about the seemingly overwhelming impact of late-game decisions, but feel that I need to play a few more games. My rating will either stay very, very high, or drop to a 5 or 6.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.8915
I really wasn't expecting to like this one at all.  It looked too much like a combination between Diplomacy and the typical generic multi-player wargame, and the rules seemed to be a bit heavy and different for what I was in the mood to play.  I haven't played very many games that require stock purchasing.  Some describe the game as having no luck.  That might actually be true.  I'm not a fan of wargames without luck, but this only wasn't too bad possibly because the wargame portion of the game is almost an afterthought.  The map, no luck, and the wooden pieces definately make me think of an updated version of Diplomacy, although I've never played that game.  However, this game feels more like Diplomacy with musical chairs as the most interesting part of the game involves switching control of the various empires between the players acting as investors.  I built up an army with France to confront Italy in North Africa then the two players that controlled France and Italy suddenly switched sides!  Everyone got to play Italy and Britain at least once, although I lost control of Britain again before I actually got to do anything with it.  I played it as a three-player game.  I think this would probably be the best way to play it.  I'm not sure how the six empires would be divided among four or five players; I'm told that things can stay rather static with six players, and I don't know that there would be much interaction with only two players.

Végre sikerült kipróbálnom a nagy elődöt és tényleg nem sok a különbség a két változat között (Imperial - Imperial 2030), de amik vannak azok mind az új kiadás felé billentik a mérleget. A térkép nehezebben átlátható, az egységek túl nagyok ezért néha alig férnek el a táblán. Jó dolog a teljes átjárhatóság, aminek hiánya ebben a régi verzióban mind az angolok, mind az oroszok helyzetét kicsit megnehezíti. Az országot nem irányító játékos helyzetét is jobban megoldották az új verzióban, de nekem az adóztatásban megjelenő különbség a legszámottevőbb. Ugyanis egyrészt ebben a kiadásban alig férnek el a bábuk az adott táblarészen, még akadályozzák is kicsit az orosz hadiflottát a táblán és macerásabb kiszámítani is a játékosnak járó bónuszt. Ez sokkal jobban ki van találva az új verzióban. Átláthatóbb, a táblán pedig jobb helyre került ennek a táblázata is.
Összességében a két játék közt nincsenek nagy különbségek, de amik vannak, azok mind az új verzióban jobbak. Egyedül talán a pénzlapok minősége jobb a régiben.
A játékban egyedül talán a hosszába tudnék belekötni. Teljesen elképzelhető, hogy nem nagyon érdeke senkinek befejezni a játékot így nagyon hosszúra is elnyúlhat, ettől függetlenül szerintem egy nagyon jó játékról van szó. Nem feltétlenül van 1 pontnyi különbség a két kiadás között, de mivel csak egészekkel pontozok így valahogy különbséget kell tennem. Akinek viszont csak ezt a verziót van lehetősége megvenni, ne habozzon, tökéletesen játszható ez is, ugyanolyan élményt adva.

RATING: 10
Wwhhhhhhheeeeeeeeeeeeeeeeeeee!  What an awesome system.  This is a brutal mindmelting goodybag.  There are so many possibilities to take into account here, who to invest in, when to try for majority or when to intend to keep minority.  There are a ton of natural objectives that come out of the design like trying to invest adjacently on the map, in order on the turn track, and/or with mutual investors.  

It seems that getting bought out often can work to your advantage.  This is really a lovely system, it feels like it took everything wonderful in this modern era of gaming and ported it into the minds of some of the best designers from the 50's - the early 70's. 

Expect to play this tactical, but know that as brutal as it is that you are in it for the long haul and what little long term strategies you can form are going to aid you if you can put everything into position.

Much like all of the 18xx games, use poker chips.  It will make the banks much easier to "read" from across the table.

Don't underestimate the option to pay to move on the rondel.  Timing is everything in this game.  Imagine in 18xx the constant potential of your trains rusting or sudden buy outs.  If you get into conflict games and stock games, this is the one for you.  


UPDATE: The more I play this the more I love it.  I do not recommend playing with 6 players though as there will be at least one player that gets squeezed out with nothing that they can do for the rest of the game.  We play with the variant where you get a buck if you don't buy stocks when without a flag.

UPDATE: The No-Investor Card variant is great as it balances the game out dramatically, but I think perhaps it takes a little more of the fun out of it and makes it a bit more deterministic.  It seems that it plays best at 4 without the Investor card and 5 with the Investor card and the C.H.O.A.M. Charity.

UPDATE: This is really a perfect 4 player game without the investor card.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Imperial is a great medium-heavy euro. It's very opaque, but the rules aren't complicated. To win players must effectively manipulate incentives, convincing and coercing others to help each other out. Full of shifting allegiances, it's a dynamic, nasty experience, so you need to pay attention on every turn. The theme is wonderfully cynical.

That said, because there's no luck, it can be a bit scripted and prone to groupthink with experienced players. There's also a bit more rules overhead and the game is longer than Chicago Express, and the endgame is a bit calculating and gamey. It's still a great design, but the game's success depends greatly on the group. Imperial is the sort of game you could and probably should replay again and again with the same group. Outside of that context, however, it suffers a bit.

RATING: 8
This is sort of like Antike (rondel, similar movement & combat, rules simplicity) combined with a stock game (shares, "operating" ie taxing to improve company/country revenue/worth). Players control 6 nations by having invested the most (most share worth). Countries can build/buy military units, develop revenue (build factory, expand territory), collect taxes (which also improves the value of the country's shares), etc. Each nation has its own treasury separate from player monies, and players must manage cash flow well.

2nd play, another 6er (4 newbies). This one dragged on for 2.5 hours, which isn't really bad! The winner somehow managed to lose his country at the right time and was then able to invest repeatedly - I'm not sure I like that, although I might if I can figure out how to do it! Still a solid game, but I wouldn't play more than 4er with newbies, to minimize between-turn downtime.

RATING: 5
This is an economic game with some light Risk-style war game/area control elements.  In this game players become controllers of different world powers and those powers themselves "take turns" with the controlling player deciding which action that world power takes.  These actions are decided using a Rondel (it is a Mac Gerdts game after-all) and consist of buying bonds in a specific country, paying out investors, and taxing the country as well as producing and moving units to take over more area.

Even though the economic elements are the lion's share of this game, I couldn't help but feeling like this game boiled down to Risk.  And I don't like Risk.  Honestly, even the whole package with the economic elements was very dry.  The game was very deep and I didn't even quite get how I should play but it didn't offer anything to draw me back in and figure it out.  There's certainly an audience for this game, I'm just not part of that audience.

RATING: 8
Very interesting game.  This game has you owning shares of countries that you buy stock in, the person with the most stock controls that country and performs that countries actions.  This little aspect makes the brutal attacking and cutthroat blocking seem less critical, since you have interest in more than one country.  What I mean is, in other wargames, if you are Germany, and you get attacked, it is a big deal and you feel the pressure of trying o defend, well in Imperial, since you only own shares in Germany, it is not such a kick in the gut when something bad happens.  So when Austria is dominating the game and kicking Germany’s ass, you can simply buy stock in Austria.  I definitely need to play this again because I am intrigued and it is deep enough that I haven’t begun to truly grasp the strategy during my one play.

I've plaed it twice now and I am really starting to enjoy the investor aspect.  I was very congniscent of rondel position in this play and I ended up in a close second.  I enjoyed this enough to pick up Imperial 2030.

****Update***

After a few more plays I have come to understand the relationships between nations and how to manipulate them more.  For example, I was in a situation where Russia was vulnerable to an Austrian arms build-up and instead of locking horns with them (with would benefit none of the principles), I instead bought the leading interest in Austria and pointed them in a different direction.  I also learned ot use the Rondel to buy into companies when they are ripe for Invest or possibly Taxation.  All in all an impressive game design.  I do liek the 2030 rule where the amount to pay for going extra spaces on the wheel scales with the power points of a country.  This prevents the leader from banging away at Tax-Ivest-Tax.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The fact that you do not have exclusive ownership of any nation takes this out of the Risk/Axis camp and makes it something new. It's as if the players are arms dealers. This game is about empire building and combat, but the point is not to dominate, it's to bet on the winner.

RATING: 7.5
[Eggert-Spiele 07 Edition] {Europe, civilization, economic; fighting, stock holding, rondel; optimal 4-6p}  Imperial is a decent stock-holding game.  It is unique in mechanic and game feel.  I quite love it.  A game turn is fast and a typical game can be finished within 2 hours (very good!).  It has no luck and is serious - it is only suitable for gamers.  The game situation is opaque; players need good skills and experiences to understand the situation and act accordingly.

It is a multifaceted game. Players can develop many strategies and tactics to get better scores.  It supports long-term strategic planning well.   A player can choose manage multiple nations or no nations at all.  It is not chaotic even with 6 players!  Player interaction is strong.  Although it is not target as a negotiation game, negotiation skill is quite important in this game.

I don't really like the battle system though it is simple and no luck.  It brings no surprises or dynamic factor to the game.  It makes a war quite dry.  I still prefer some randomness in a battle.

The components are effective and sturdy.  However, the borders on the board are not clear enough.  I like the wooden pawns - sturdy and nicely shaped :)  The rulebook is not professionally proof-read.  There are typos and ambiguities.

RATING: 5.5
I wanted to like Imperial. I did. I had caught a whiff of the hype on BGG about it and thought that the Rondel sounded neat and some of the other mechanics could be cool. So I asked a friend to bring it to a recent game night, and we gave it a play. There was mass confusion at first as we struggled through the rules--even the people who'd played once or twice were having some trouble refreshing themselves--but the game flow was pretty simple once we got the hang of it. It's largely a war game with economic aspects in how it plays out (not in its win conditions, but we'll get there later), and I'm not much of a war game person, so it didn't grab me much there. The fact that we could steal control of countries from each other and, indeed, make it so someone really didn't have a turn at all was interesting. I mostly contented myself with running Germany into the ground (did I mention that I'm bad at war games?) and buying stocks from whatever country seemed to be doing well at the moment.

And... I won. I managed to [i]accomplish absolutely nothing of value[/i] the entire game, and still came in first place. And while I find that interesting, I also feel like it's a very cheap win. Yes, you can argue that I handled the game exactly as I should have, and that the others who were doing better on the war-game front had lost sight of the actual victory conditions, or whatever other argument you want to posit. But in the end, I just don't feel like it was a satisfying win for me, or probably for anyone else at the table. And I'd probably let that slide if I enjoyed the actual gameplay more, but I didn't. The Rondel was neat but nothing earth-shattering, and I'm just not big on moving armies around. I don't have a head for that sort of game and find myself just sort of blandly going through the motions when I play such. I'm sure for the right sort of person, Imperial is AWESOME. But I'm not that person.

RATING: 7
Rondel-driven game set during the age of European imperialism. Players purchase bonds in nations (there are six of these in the game), with the person holding the largest stake controlling the actions of that country. Actions consist of constructing factories, building and manoeuvring military units, taxing factories and conquered territories etc. There are two distinct forms of currency in the game, a nation's funds, which is used to build it's infrastructure and to pay out dividends to investors, and the money belonging to the investors (players), which is used to increase their investments in particular countries. The investment aspect of the game is the one real flaw to by found in the gameplay as a player requires possession of the investment card and only then can shares be purchased. This brings an element of luck to the game as players ahead of others in turn order can invest in the more powerful nations, and it can be frustrating not to be able to invest despite having sufficient funds due to a quirk of the game. 'Imperial' is a very well devised game and there's a lot going on here to admire in terms of gameplay (I particularly like how ownership of the nations can change hands several times in a game resulting in players having to quickly re-adapt their strategies), but generally I get a sense of there being a little too much being in the right place at the right time (just like history actually) that prevents this from being a classic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One play with the investor card. That was a pretty interesting experience.  I really liked the opportunities presented by the fact that you can invest in the various countries.  If a country is threatening you, just invest to a controlling stake and send the country's forces elsewhere.  Lots of neat ideas.  I like that most of the battles for gain are over neutral countries and you never really loose your starting provinces.  Someone can occupy your country but it only hurts you and they don't gain anything.  I also like the various actions to make the countries money and turn the money into player money.  The scoring was also interesting.  I have some reservations that the action on the roundel is a little linear. Conclusion: my rating could rise after playing the advanced version which changes the investing. 2008/03/23

RATING: 10
Zseniális játék. Szeretem a fordított gondolkodást igénylő játékokat (lásd még: Last Will). Remek ötlet és eleddig a legjobb rotás játék, amit ismerek. Az országok vezetésével nem az egyes birodalmak javát kell nézni, hanem kizárólag a saját javunkat és csak ez alapján szabad döntést hozni. Aki kibillen a szerepből és érzelmileg elköteleződik egy ország vagy egy befektetés mellett, az menthetetlenül bukik.
A diplomáciának és a részvénybirtoklási (országirányítási) stratégiának kiemelt a jelentősége. Egy-egy birodalmat többféleképpen is lehet az érdekeinknek megfelelően fel/kihasználni. 
5-6 fővel nem érdemes játszani, mert a játékélményt mégiscsak az nyújtja, ha tevékenyen részt veszünk az országok irányításában és ekkora játékosszámnál, már könnyebben előfordul, hogy "kiesnek" a játékból némelyek. 4 fővel tökéletes az egyensúly és kellően pörgős a játékmenet, ugyanakkor az interakciók változatossága is megvan. RATING: 8
A Winsome-like train game disguised as an area control/war game. Better than almost any stock-and-rails game save for maybe Chicago Express, and certainly better than your average 18xx. 

Opaque, but not so much that it's completely out of reach for new players. But get caught up in the area-control trap and you'll lose to someone who is just making the best investments.

A cynical game in the best possible way, and a game I would call a quintessential gaming experience.

RATING: 9
Mac Gerdts is defintely turning into one of my favorite game designers. Importing the magnifically done action-choice-system from his very own Antike,Imperial comes up with a very important as well as very interesting difference. You don't control one nation(or colour), you invest in as many of the nations as you want to. Only the person who invested the most into a country determines the path of that nation. This method is bound for several role changes throughout the game. Since one is very likely to have invested into severals countries it is quite suspenseful to see what kind of destiny others choose for a nation you are also involved in. Terrific game! Everyone in the group loved it.

RATING: 9
Upgraded after playing with no imperial card and 4 players, loved it.
This game is quite unique to me. First timers will find it challenging to not think of themselves as "being a country".  You invest and improve to raise your share value and lower others, in part by military might.  But you win or lose based on half a dozen key purchases of "investments".  Talking with a GM who led 7 back-to-back games at Strategicon and observing 2 games I have a huge respect for the mechanics the game provides you, including the ability to stop otherwise runaway leaders in their tracks.

RATING: 10
What a fantastic game!  It dramatically improves upon its predecessor Antike by employing the same Rondel mechanic but implementing it in a significantly better game.  The notion of everyone investing in any of the six Great Powers of Europe, and the largest investor at any one time being able to control the actions of that country is very innovative and clever.  The game seems to constantly be in flux as people shift around who they control and what their short-term goals are, but in a good way because it keeps you constantly engaged and interested whether or not it's your turn.  I really look forward to playing again and may pick up a copy at some point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very enjoyable game. I think the rondel mechanism works very well with the stock-market-like control of countries. I also like very much that there is no way to ditch a bond in a country you have bought -- pick your horses carefully. I think a key idea is return on investment -- you want to be sure that your purchases pay dividends out to you before you see the Investor card again, so be wary of buying more stock in the country that just hit Investor. The dynamic start is definitely preferred. It allows the players' initial perception of countries' prospects to play more of a role, as well as allowing players to make plays for controlling two adjacent countries (generally a powerful strategy). I think it's certainly appropriate for more experienced players, but I don't think there's so much to choose between the countries that agamer with no Imperial experience would be hugely disadvantaged; it's more a question of the dynamics leading to certain countries being big or small, than of some countries being inherently better. (Although I do think that Britain may have a bit of a leg up if it chooses to gothe build-many-factories and be peaceful route.) 

Potential for 10-status if I can get enough plays in.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Got this to see if it was Princes of the Renaissance "done right" (i.e. more control over what your actions do) and to see if the Rondel is of interest.

...Finally played this. It's a commodity speculation game where the commodities are six of the seven Diplomacy countries (no Turkey). It has no luck, and definitely choices seem more meaningful than in PotR.

The Rondel in fact is not too bad. I thought I might not like it because I'm used to the collective Rondel of Santiago de Cuba, where players move the same token and this is a source of interaction. In Imperial everyone has their own marker on the Rondel and there is no interaction there, but the actions you take are quite interactive (whereas in SdC you're probably just converting cubes), so it's a wash.

My main thoughts are that the game is too long, and the "narrative" from a play-through seems arbitrary or incoherent (hard to summarize and explain what was happening). What you actually do to play is simple, but it takes a long time to think through moves and evaluate where players stand.

The game makes me want to design a variant with the same skeleton but even simpler details. When I'm playing, I'm quite interested to see what will happen, but it seems to take so long to get there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Rating based on two games with five players. Be warned, this game should not be compared to Antike at all. Where Antike can be compared to any "light Civ" game, Imperial feels like a crossover between 1830 and specifically Mare Nostrum. It feels most like 1830 though. In addition, although both games are not too difficult to explain, Antike's rules are still easier to grasp, turns are still a bit quicker and gametime is probably a little less as well. With a bit more of a gamer's crowd, however, Imperial is much more cut-throat and offers even more strategies for winning. The game requires strategic as well as tactical decisions.
UPDATE: I have a serious issue with the very limited number of times you can react to a changed situation on the board by purchasing new bonds. Too bad.

RATING: 6
Update: After 8 plays, with and without the investor card, my issues with Imperial have overtaken my interest in it. Your success in the game is determined too much by turn order and timing issues, and the whims of other players.

Imperial is one of the least satisfying games that I am most willing to play. It's such a great concept and is so full of interesting parts, but seems to always have problems and frustrating elements. I think basically the stock-purchasing decisions are too constrained for a stock game. You are limited to the available bonds, you only get to invest every once in a while, and you may not sell bonds. I also don't like so much the end-game taxation rush and that not everyone gets to invest at the end of the game.

After many games with the investor card, we tried one without it and, while promising at first, it ended up having its own problematic issues.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fantastic.  Holds my attention from start to finish like few other games manage.  Unlike [gameid=19600], whose use of the rondel feels a little bit unpolished, this time around the rondel provides some very tense decisions.  Love the tactical puzzles that this game provides, coupled with the necessity of ignoring position on the board to make sure that you're still getting your money's worth out of the country.  Fine balance between stripping the country of too many resources, and making it too flush and powerful and sinking too many resources into it without payback.  There's a tension that is palpable in the room when this game comes out.  The threat of direct conflict casts a shadow, and there's lots of number-crunching that leads to a very heavy, engaging atmosphere.  It doesn't bother me, but if you like your games lighthearted, approach with caution.  If you have issues with one or more players getting shut out of play by losing their last nation and not having any spare cash to buy back into the lead in anything I would suggest using the Swiss Bank and no Investor card variants.  The Swiss Bank variant gets people who are out of the game back in, and taking away the Investor card takes away the possibility of the Swiss Bank winning by hoovering up all the small bonds while the other players all sit helpless.  And, if you are curious, I prefer this original to [gameid=54138], as the fewer neutrals and different power gaining track feels tighter.
