=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It looks like Risk but it's actually more like Acquire. I played with 6 which is probably the best way to go although I'm told 5 works well also. There are 6 countries and each one takes a turn in order. The player who "controls" that country when its turn comes up is the person who makes the decision as to what that country will do; tax, invest, build a factory, produce armies/ships, maneuver/attack with armies and ships, import armies. The "controller" is the person with the most bonds in that country. Bonds are purchased during the investment phase and victory points are accumalted during the taxation phase. The game ends when a country hits 25 points. Victory points are multiplied by bonds owned   money in hand. Our game was run by someone very familiar with the game and a player who had played only once before. The other 4 were newbs. The game took over 4 hours. I would think with experienced players the game would clock in around 2.5 hours. There is a lot of game in that time. A lot of negotiating as well which I'm not a huge fan of. I finished 18 points behind the victor (our teacher) and only 4 points behind 2nd place. The person who had played once before finished 5th. I would definitely try this again and reevaluate my rating. *Update 1/15/09* With the new BGG 'plays best with' poll it shows that 6 is NOT the best way to go. 4 is optimal.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Imperial is a stock market game where you are investing in various WWI era nations, capitalizing on their wars and then investing somewhere else when your favored nation starts to run out of steam. It takes a bit to figure out exactly what you are supposed to be doing, but once everyone puzzles out the rhythm of expansion and exploitation, the game takes off. 

Understanding that pattern makes the reasons for war become more clear. Players start to anticipate when a nation is going to cash in on its holdings, or when a nation's controller is about to cash out the treasury. Once everyone at the table connects those dots, the game becomes a pressure cooker as you try to skim as much money as possible without setting up your opponents to do the same. There is absolutely nothing else like it in my collection, nor is there really anything else like it in the war game arena.

It's a stone cold classic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A cynical game where players are cast as a Gnome of Zürich in old Europe and political influence is a salable commodity.  The player with the most political influence in a nation controls the nation's foreign and domestic policy:  build and deploy armies/navies, build factories, wage war, collect taxes, and/or collect tribute.  The game can feel a bit like musical chairs in regards to control of nations.  Sometimes a player controls multiple nations, other times they control none.  The player who most heavily invested in the winningest nations and avoided the losers is victorious.

Selecting a nation's action via the Rondel feels satisfying and keeps the game moving.  Despite much of the mechanics being fairly straightforward, a winning strategy is tough to craft.  What makes it tough for me is that control of any nation is rarely firm and it's the nations that take turns, not the players themselves.  Also, scoring happens only at the conclusion which makes it tougher to gauge one's standing during play.

RATING: 8
Very impressed. I love the quasi-combination of wargame, diplomacy and stock exchange. Novel elements blend together well to create a game with familiar elements that still feels fresh and engaging. I actually like the Investor rules as written (so far) and don't see the appeal of removing it -- making the control of countries more static seems to undermine part of the game's novelty and appeal. 

What keeps this from being higher is the arduous endgame, where 1 of X different players/countries could end the game -- so it's necessary to count up points based on several different ending scenarios to see what the best move is... This can take a very long time, artifically dragging out the end of what is otherwise a brisk, tense game.

Also, having played with and without the investor card, I simply cannot imagine why some people prefer to play without the card - the added time alone should discourage people from that variant... not to mention the stale, static progress of the game. Oh, and never ever ever every play where you can simply grab $1 from the bank if you have no flags - it completely encourages the first player to lose control of a country to run the bank dry and purchase 2nd place in each country...

RATING: 10
+: quite easy to learn the rules (not to master the game!)
+: solid gamers-only strategy game
+: unique
+: area movement simply appeals to me (more than Hamburgum and Princes of Macchu Picchu)
+: little randomness (except for player order (bid?))
+: several officially promoted free (!) variants
+: predict the choices of other players
+: very flexible number of players
+: playable in 1-1.5 hours at BSW (vs. tough players)
-?: winning potential can depend a bit on player chaos. Player movement on rondel affects when you can buy, and how rich you are at that time. 
-?: 'winning' without controlling is a real (and boring) possibility! (tentative)
-: poses a steep learning curve: winning condition can appear counterintuitive during first plays (money, not domination)
-: runaway leader possibility so you can be "out of the game" if you played poorly (or got unlucky) and may need to sit it out. Good players will then do some leaderbashing.
-: when others have AP :-(
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I didn't think I'd like Imperial despite liking Antike as the idea of losing "my" civilization didn't appeal to me.  It turns out that a stock holding game crossed with a wargame is totally brilliant and I ordered my own copy as soon as I got home from the Con where I first played... and only waited that long since nobody had a copy in the vendor hall!

Trying to invest in the countries best positioned for success is really interesting as is figuring out the best way to be successful with the countries you are running.  Do you roll over and let something else you are heavily invested in steal the one your runnings points?  Or do you invest heavily in yourself and keep your empire running strong?  Or gain control of a couple countries and do mutually beneficial things?  The fluid nature of the game is very interesting and unique and I'm looking forward to exploring Imperial a lot more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Finally a rondel game that I can appreciate.  However, it wasn't because of the signature mechanism, but rather the clever use of the limited investing, as well as the unpredictability of the country ownership, which really made the difference for me.  Unlike Antike, which I found incredibly lacking due to its endgame being reduced to a arms race to squeeze out those last few VP needed to win the game, the military aspect in Imperial is much better implemented in comparison.  The agony in the decision of which nation to invest in; of weighing current vs. future prospects; of whether to consolidate your control or to jump ship to a more lucrative target.  These are the choices that make the game wrought with tension and fun.

RATING: 10
If there ever was a perfect and 100% streamlined strategic design... Risk, Monopoly and Diplomacy as they should've been from the beginning – blended into perfection. I elaborated my opinion in thorough here: https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1272624/review-croatian-recenzija-igre-na-hrvatskom-jeziku
but it's in Croatian :-D
I lowered the grade to 9 recently because my group was more than once frustrated with "forcing option" (as we call it) on rondel and the option was made more expensive in other rondel games so designer himself obviously thought it should have been the same with Imperial, but after some thought – this one deserves perfect 10 – and for long it was my only 10 (Here I Stand got it more as a 10 experience than as a 10 game). It has its beginning, development and endgame and when to start an endgame, when to start "forcing" is entirely up to your judgment. That justifies "forcing" for me. 

Constantly replayed.

RATING: 8

RATING: 5
After 2+ Plays: I like the rondel mechanism, but I had the same problem with this as I did with 18xx.  It feels like a stock game, and so while the actions on the board are secondary, they still take up most of the playing time.  If I'm going to play a game about manipulating stocks, I'd prefer to focus my energies on that aspect and not on a very complicated board situation that only really matters in how it changes stock prices.  If that makes any sense to you, and you want to try an investing game with more focus on the investing, try [gameid=5] or [gameid=94].
